# Troop Leader Guidebook Vol 1

# Scouts BSA Troop Leader Guidebook Volume 1

## Introduction: Welcome to Scouts BSA

Scouts BSA is a results-oriented program for developing leaders of character. The program is only as strong as you make it. Your leadership and enthusiasm will give your Scouts all the support you need so you can give your Scouts all the opportunities they deserve.

### Support Resources

Essential BSA publications for troop leaders:
- **Scouts BSA Handbook for Girls/Boys** - Primary resource for Scouts
- **Fieldbook** - Advanced outdoor skills
- **Troop Leader Guidebook (Volumes 1 and 2)** - Resources for Scoutmasters and assistant Scoutmasters
- **Troop Committee Guidebook** - Primary resource for troop committee members
- **Program Features and Outing Ideas (Volumes 1, 2, and 3)** - Monthly program features and meeting plans
- **Scouts BSA Requirements** - Requirements for ranks, merit badges, and special awards
- **Guide to Uniforms and Insignia** - Guide to wearing uniforms and badges correctly
- **Guide to Advancement** - Official source for administering advancement
- **Guide to Safe Scouting** - Primary source for conducting activities safely
- **Patrol Leader Handbook** - Official guide for patrol leaders
- **Senior Patrol Leader Handbook** - Official guide for senior patrol leaders

## Scout Basics

### Mission, Aims, Methods, Ideals

Scouts BSA serves youth aged 10 or 11 through 18. Its mission is to prepare young people to make ethical and moral choices over their lifetimes by instilling in them the values of the Scout Oath and Scout Law.

#### The Aims of Scouts BSA

**Character Development:** Character is what someone does when no one is looking. It is the set of ethical and moral principles that guide a person's every decision.

**Citizenship Training:** True citizens accept the responsibilities of citizenship as quickly as they claim the rights that citizenship confers. A Scout troop is a laboratory of democracy.

**Leadership:** A leader steps up in times of crisis and is able to influence others to accomplish a specific goal. Scouting encourages youth to learn and practice leadership skills.

**Mental and Physical Fitness:** Scouting promotes mental and physical fitness through a rigorous program of outdoor activities and merit badges that encourage youth to stretch both their muscles and their minds.

#### The Methods of Scouts BSA

**Adult Association:** Youth need positive role models in the form of Scoutmasters and assistant Scoutmasters, merit badge counselors, and other caring adults.

**Advancement:** Scouts BSA's ranks, merit badges, and special awards recognize Scouts for learning skills and taking on increased responsibility.

**Leadership Development:** The ideal troop is led by youth who are guided by adults. Through formal training, informal coaching, and practical experience, young leaders train others and manage projects.

**Outdoors:** Youth join Scouting to have fun. The program is built around outdoor activities. All troop meetings should be devoted to learning outdoor skills and getting ready for the next outing.

**Patrol Method:** Each troop is made up of one or more patrols: groups of about eight Scouts who camp, cook, play, and learn together.

**Personal Growth:** Assuring that the troop creates opportunities for growth through individual Good Turns and patrol and troop service projects.

**Uniform:** The Scout uniform demonstrates unity, minimizes differences among Scouts, and serves as a wearable display of achievements.

**Ideals:** The real heart of Scouting is laid out in the Scout Oath, Scout Law, Scout motto, and Scout slogan.

#### The Ideals of Scouting

**Scout Oath:**
On my honor I will do my best
To do my duty to God and my country and to obey the Scout Law;
To help other people at all times;
To keep myself physically strong, mentally awake, and morally straight.

**Scout Law:**
A Scout is trustworthy, loyal, helpful, friendly, courteous, kind, obedient, cheerful, thrifty, brave, clean, and reverent.

**Scout Motto:** Be Prepared.

**Scout Slogan:** Do a Good Turn Daily.

## Troop Structure

Every troop has four basic elements: the chartered organization, the Scouts, the leaders, and the troop committee.

### The Chartered Organization

The Boy Scouts of America grants annual charters to local religious institutions, schools, civic organizations, and similar groups. Each chartered organization agrees to:
- Provide a safe meeting place
- Include Scouting as part of its overall program for youth and families
- Appoint a chartered organization representative
- Select a troop committee
- Provide adequate facilities for meetings
- Encourage outdoor experiences

### The Chartered Organization Representative

The chartered organization representative heads the organization's Scouting department and serves as a liaison between the organization and its Scouting units.

### The Troop Committee

The troop committee functions as a board of directors and parent support group. It sets troop policies and handles administrative functions. At minimum, it consists of one chairperson and two committee members.

Typical committee positions include:
- Chairperson
- Advancement coordinator
- Chaplain
- Equipment coordinator
- Membership coordinator
- Outdoor/activities coordinator
- Parent communication coordinator
- Training coordinator
- Treasurer
- Parent coordinator

### The Scoutmaster Corps

The Scoutmaster and assistant Scoutmasters work directly with the Scouts. Having assistant Scoutmasters is strongly recommended for all but the smallest troops.

### Youth Membership Requirements

Youth can join Scouts BSA if they are at least 11 years old, are in the fifth grade and register on or after March 1, or have earned the Arrow of Light Award and are at least 10 years old and under age 18.

### Adult Leadership Requirements

To serve as a registered adult leader, one must:
- Abide by the Scout Oath, Scout Law, and Scouter Code of Conduct
- Subscribe to the precepts of the Declaration of Religious Principle
- Reside within the USA or a U.S. territory, or be a U.S. citizen residing outside the USA
- Be 21 or older for primary leadership positions
- Be 18 or older for assistant leadership positions
- Complete Youth Protection training before application is processed and renew it every two years
- Complete background check process
- Take leader position-specific training

## Patrol Method

The patrol is a semi-permanent group of compatible Scouts who elect their own leader. Each patrol has its own name, uniform emblem, and yell.

### Types of Patrols

**New-Scout Patrols:** Scouts who have just joined the troop. The troop assigns a "troop guide" and an assistant Scoutmaster to help with advancement.

**Regular Patrols:** Members have similar interests and perhaps spend time together outside the troop. They typically serve Scouts who have reached First Class rank.

**Older-Scout Patrols:** Serve older Scouts who want to pursue more challenging high-adventure outings while maintaining their connection to the troop.

### Patrol Size

The ideal patrol size is eight members. Eight Scouts can hike and camp together efficiently while minimizing their impact on the environment.

### Building Patrol Spirit

Patrol spirit starts with the patrol name, which the members choose. Strong patrol identity is built through:
- Pitting patrols against each other in games and competitions
- Recognizing patrols that do well in uniform or complete inspections
- Rewarding the patrol with the best overall attendance
- Giving patrol awards

## The Troop in Context

### The Local Council

The BSA works through approximately 350 local councils across the country. These councils provide:
- Training and support for troop leaders
- General liability insurance
- Camping facilities
- A service center
- Professional staff assistance

### The District

Most local councils are divided into geographic districts. The district is responsible for four functions:

**Membership:** Growing Scouting by organizing new units, recruiting new members, and retaining current members.

**Program:** Supporting units by promoting camping and community service and challenging Scouts to advance.

**Finance:** Providing funds to support council operations.

**Unit Services:** Providing direct coaching and consultation to help units succeed.

### District Committee

The district committee includes:
- District Membership Committee
- District Program Committee
- District Activities and Civic Service Committee
- District Training Committee
- District Advancement and Recognition Committee

### Commissioner Staff

**Unit Commissioner:** Responsible for supporting three or so units. Can serve as a representative, teacher, coach, or problem-solver.

**Roundtable Commissioner:** Hosts monthly roundtable meetings that bring together leaders to network, learn, and find solutions.

## Uniforms and Insignia

The uniform is one of the eight methods of Scouting. When worn properly, it establishes uniformity across troop members and serves as a wearable trophy case.

### Uniform Basics

The full field uniform consists of:
- Scout long-sleeved or short-sleeved shirt
- Scout pants or shorts
- Scout socks or knee socks
- BSA cap or campaign hat (troop optional)
- Scout belt and buckle
- Scout neckerchief, slide tie, or neckwear (troop optional)

### Encouraging Uniforming

The best way to encourage proper uniforming is to make sure adult leaders set a good example. Wear your complete uniform to all troop meetings and recognize the best-uniformed patrol.

### When Not to Wear the Uniform

Do not wear the uniform:
- While selling a commercial product or service
- While engaged in any activity that could dishonor the BSA
- As a costume or disguise
- In situations that might imply BSA endorsement of political candidates
- While participating in public demonstrations or protests

## The Scouts BSA Program

### The Annual Program Cycle

Scouting follows a year-round program cycle that includes:
- Weekly troop meetings
- Monthly outings
- Summer camp for all Scouts
- High-adventure trips for older Scouts
- Courts of honor three or four times a year
- Service projects and fundraisers
- Annual planning conference

### The Monthly Program Cycle

Each month typically includes:
- Four weekly troop meetings
- One monthly outing
- Patrol leaders' council meeting
- Troop committee meeting

## Troop Program Planning

Effective planning prevents boring meetings and unorganized outings. Planning includes both long-term planning (annual planning conference) and short-term planning (monthly patrol leaders' council meetings).

### Long-Term Planning

Long-term planning happens at the annual planning conference, typically held in late spring or early summer. The process includes:

1. Gathering necessary information (school dates, community events, etc.)
2. Discussing the planning process with the senior patrol leader
3. Setting goals for the troop
4. Inviting appropriate people to attend the conference

### Holding the Annual Planning Conference

The conference should result in an annual calendar listing all monthly program features, dates for activities, and brief descriptions. Steps include:

1. Discuss troop goals for the next 12 months
2. Review draft calendar with important dates
3. Discuss what Scouts want to do each month
4. Add courts of honor, service projects, and other important dates
5. Take final vote for approval
6. Share the plan with troop families and chartered organization

### Short-Term Planning

At each monthly patrol leaders' council meeting, leaders plan activities for the following month's troop meetings using a three-month approach:
- Review the month that is ending
- Plan in detail the month that is beginning
- Preview the following month

## Troop Meetings

Troop meetings serve multiple purposes:
- **Exercising leadership** - Scouts plan and conduct meetings
- **Building community** - Patrols gather together and compete
- - **Promoting advancement** - Individual Scouts complete requirements
- **Inspiring Scouts** - Ceremonies emphasize Scouting ideals
- **Having fun** - Games and activities keep meetings enjoyable

### The Parts of a Troop Meeting

1. **Preopening (15 minutes)** - Setup, huddles, flag ceremony preparation
2. **Opening (10 minutes)** - Flag ceremony, Scout Oath and Law
3. **Group instruction (10 minutes)** - Skills needed for upcoming outings
4. **Skills development (35 minutes)** - Specific skills practice by groups
5. **Patrol meetings (15 minutes)** - Patrol business and planning
6. **Interpatrol activity (15 minutes)** - Competition between patrols
7. **Closing (5 minutes)** - Announcements, recognition, Scoutmaster's Minute
8. **After the meeting (15 minutes)** - Cleanup and informal discussion

### Using Adult Leaders at Troop Meetings

Adults should:
- Give the Scoutmaster's Minute
- Assist with skills instruction if qualified
- Keep meetings on schedule
- Help patrols with specific skills
- Touch base with individual Scouts
- Counsel on merit badges and sign off requirements
- Check in with youth leader counterparts
- Watch for Scouts who are not involved

Adults should never:
- Run the meeting
- Criticize or call out Scouts publicly
- Jump in to take over from youth leaders

## The Outdoor Program

The outdoor program is where real Scouting happens. While outdoor skills may be learned indoors, camping is one of the most powerful methods for achieving Scouting's aims.

### Scouting's Continuum of Outdoor Activities

Outdoor activities must be age-appropriate:
- New Scouts: activities like hiking to nearby campsites
- Experienced Scouts: backpacking trips, canoe trips, more distant locations
- Older Scouts: high-adventure activities with minimum age requirements

### Planning Patrol Activities

With proper training, guidance, and approval, patrols can plan short hikes and service projects following these rules:
- The Scoutmaster must approve the patrol activity
- The patrol activity cannot interfere with any troop function
- Follow BSA safety policies including two-deep leadership

### Program Hazard Analysis and Safety

Use these tools for safe activities:
- **Program Hazard Analysis** - Process to identify risks and protective measures
- **Safety Checklists** - Inspect vehicles, meeting places, and equipment
- **Safety PAUSE** - Last-minute safety check before beginning activities

### Outdoor Ethics

#### The Outdoor Code
*As an American, I will do my best to—*
- *Be clean in my outdoor manners.*
- *Be careful with fire.*
- *Be considerate in the outdoors.*
- *Be conservation-minded.*

#### Leave No Trace Principles

1. **Plan Ahead and Prepare**
2. **Travel and Camp on Durable Surfaces**
3. **Dispose of Waste Properly** - Pack it in, pack it out
4. **Leave What You Find**
5. **Minimize Campfire Impacts**
6. **Respect Wildlife**
7. **Be Considerate of Other Visitors**

#### Tread Lightly! Principles

- **Travel Responsibly** - Stay on designated roads and trails
- **Respect the Rights of Others** - Allow others to enjoy solitude and safety
- **Educate Yourself** - Study maps and regulations
- **Avoid Sensitive Areas** - Stay away from wildlife habitats and fragile areas

### Types of Outings

The Guide to Safe Scouting includes activities that are unauthorized (boxing, skydiving) and activities requiring special training and/or certification.

### Planning Outings

Basic planning includes:
- Determining leadership needs (minimum two registered adult leaders 21+)
- Setting dates and locations
- Planning activities and schedules
- Preparing for rainy-day activities
- Securing necessary equipment

### Menus and Shopping

Camp cooking should be done by patrols. Each patrol plans its own meals and handles shopping. The patrol leader and one other Scout typically handle purchasing within the budget set by the patrol leaders' council.

### Personal Gear

#### Scout Basic Essentials
- Backpack
- First-aid kit
- Flashlight
- Rain gear
- Water bottle
- Trail food
- Matches and fire starters
- Sun protection
- Map and compass

For overnight camping, add:
- Clothing appropriate for season
- Sleeping bag
- Sleeping pad
- Ground cloth
- Eating kit
- Toiletries kit
- Plastic garbage bags
- Nylon cord (50 feet)

### Electronic Devices and Outings

Develop a troop policy for electronic devices that considers both their useful features and the need to maintain the outdoor experience and group cohesion.

## Service Projects

Scouting has a long heritage of community service. Service projects help Scouts complete advancement requirements and demonstrate Scouting's value to the community.

### Frequency and Scheduling

Troops should complete service projects during the year, at least one of which benefits the chartered organization. Projects require two-deep registered leadership like any other Scout activity.

### Planning Effective Service Projects

Service projects should:
- **Be real and significant** - Make a real difference
- **Be democratic** - Scouts participate in selecting and planning
- **Be clearly defined** - Have definite beginning and ending points
- **Be well prepared** - Plan thoroughly in advance
- **Be followed by reflection and recognition** - Process the experience

## Ceremonies

Ceremonies mark important moments and should be:
- Interesting and joyful
- As brief as possible
- Well planned and practiced
- Scout-planned and Scout-led
- Free of embarrassment or hazing
- Focused on honor and interests of Scouts

### Types of Ceremonies

**Opening and Closing Ceremonies:** Signal start and end of meetings, typically include flag ceremonies and Scout Oath or Law.

**Patriotic Ceremonies:** Emphasize respect for the flag and country.

**Investiture and Graduation Ceremonies:** Welcome new members to the troop.

**Installation Ceremonies:** Formalize new youth leaders taking office.

**Courts of Honor:** Formal recognition of Scouts who have advanced in rank, held three or four times per year.

**Worship Services:** Led by chaplain aide, usually interfaith services appropriate for all Scout faiths.

## Understanding and Working With Youth

Understanding adolescent development helps Scout leaders work more effectively with youth at different stages.

### Ages and Stages

**Late Childhood (Age 10):**
- Still children
- Beginning to transfer feelings from parents to other adults
- Looking to friends for emotional support
- Operate in groups rather than having best friends
- Beginning to push boundaries

**Early Adolescence (Ages 11-13):**
- Going through puberty
- Growing rapidly at uneven rates
- Identifying more with peer group than parents
- Can be moody and struggle with emotional control
- Beginning to develop prefrontal cortex for reasoning

**Middle Adolescence (Ages 14-15):**
- Full throes of puberty
- Dating or interested in dating
- Loosening ties with parents
- May engage in risky behavior
- Better able to think abstractly than before

**Late Adolescence (Ages 16-18):**
- Look more like adults than children
- Aware decisions affect their futures
- Want to prove independence
- More comfortable with academic and social status
- Can reason nearly as well as adults but lack life experience

### Techniques for Working With Youth

- **Meet Scouts where they are** - Recognize developmental levels
- **Be friendly and respectful** - Treat Scouts as you want to be treated
- **Be consistent, firm, and fair** - Set clear expectations
- **Focus on the positive** - Praise publicly, criticize privately

### Teaching Values

#### Scoutmaster's Minutes
Brief thoughts about values shared at the end of meetings. Should be short, teach a single clear lesson, and may use props.

#### Teachable Moments
Use unexpected situations to help Scouts learn through open-ended questions rather than lectures.

#### Ethical Dilemmas
Present situations where values conflict and guide Scouts through thinking about difficult decisions.

#### Reflection
Help Scouts assess experiences and understand their meaning through careful questioning and discussion.

## Working With Youth Leaders

Youth leadership development is among the most challenging and rewarding aspects of being a Scoutmaster.

### Meeting Youth Leaders Where They Are

Understand what training and experience youth leaders have had and provide appropriate support - like teaching a child to ride a bicycle, you cannot just "let go" without preparation.

### Personal Coaching

New youth leaders should meet with adult leaders to:
- Understand their position and responsibilities
- Receive clear expectations
- Get assurance of support and guidance

### Ongoing Coaching

Each youth leader should have an adult counterpart who mentors throughout their term by:
- Helping set goals
- Offering specific feedback
- Providing on-the-job training
- Touching base before activities
- Ensuring recognition for good work

### The Youth Training Continuum

**Introduction to Leadership Skills for Troops (ILST):** Six modules covering troop organization, core leadership skills, and advanced concepts. Approximately 12 hours total.

**National Youth Leadership Training (NYLT):** Week-long advanced course for Scouts who have completed ILST, are at least 13 years old, and First Class rank.

**National Advanced Youth Leadership Experience (NAYLE):** Weekend program at high-adventure bases for Scouts who have completed NYLT and are at least 14 years old.

**Den Chief Training:** For Scouts who work with Cub Scout dens, available online or as classroom course.

**Powder Horn:** Resource management course for conservation skills and outdoor leadership, open to Scouts 13+ who have completed eighth grade.

### Youth Leader Position Descriptions

Clear position descriptions and adult counterparts help youth leaders succeed. Key positions include:

**Senior Patrol Leader:**
- Preside at all troop meetings and events
- Chair the patrol leaders' council
- Meet regularly with Scoutmaster
- Assign duties to other youth leaders
- Set good example and enforce Scout behavior

**Assistant Senior Patrol Leader:**
- Train and lead appointed youth leaders
- Help lead meetings and activities
- Guide troop in senior patrol leader's absence

**Patrol Leader:**
- Plan and conduct patrol meetings
- Keep patrol members informed
- Represent patrol at patrol leaders' council meetings
- Help patrol participate in troop activities

**Troop Guide:**
- Introduce new Scouts to troop operations
- Help new Scouts reach First Class in first year
- Coach new-Scout patrol leader

Additional positions include Assistant Patrol Leader, Den Chief, Historian, Order of the Arrow Troop Representative, Librarian, Quartermaster, Scribe, Instructor, Chaplain Aide, Webmaster, Outdoor Ethics Guide, and Junior Assistant Scoutmaster.

## Recruiting and Retention

Constant recruitment is necessary as Scouts eventually leave due to aging out, moving, or losing interest.

### Arrow of Light-to-Scout Transition

The best recruitment opportunity comes from Cub Scouts who have earned Arrow of Light. The Arrow of Light program specifically prepares youth for Scouts BSA through required adventures that introduce patrol method, camping, service, fitness, and citizenship.

### Transition Process Timeline

**August:** Adult assignments for recruiting
**September:** Build relationships with Arrow of Light leaders
**February-March:** Arrow of Light Scouts attend troop meetings
**March:** Transition night and welcome campout
**May:** Focus on advancement rather than more recruiting
**June-July:** All new Scouts attend summer camp
**August:** Continue advancement and integration

### One-on-One Recruiting

Encourage every Scout to bring a friend. Strategies include:
- Visitor-friendly outings
- "Bring a friend" weekends
- Exempting recruiting Scouts from chores
- Recognizing successful recruiters
- BSA insurance covers prospects at activities

### Troop Open Houses

Special events combining individual and troop-level recruiting efforts. Include:
- Friendly greeting and name tags
- Pairing prospects with current Scouts
- Displays and demonstrations
- Parent briefings
- Food and applications
- Follow-up within one week

### Integrating New Scouts

New Scout integration requires completing numerous tasks:
- Membership application and fees
- Health forms and resource surveys
- Joining a patrol and receiving patrol emblem
- Meeting key leaders
- Understanding uniform and equipment needs
- Completing Scout rank
- Signing up for summer camp

## Adult Leader Roles and Responsibilities

Adult leaders must meet specific requirements and complete proper training to effectively support the Scouts BSA program.

### Adult Leadership Requirements

All adult leaders must:
- Abide by Scout Oath, Scout Law, and Scouter Code of Conduct
- Be registered with the BSA and subscribe to Declaration of Religious Principle
- Reside in USA/territories or be U.S. citizen abroad
- Be 21+ for primary positions, 18+ for assistant positions
- Complete Youth Protection Training before approval and renew every two years
- Complete background check process
- Take position-specific training

### Essentials of Scout Leadership

Effective Scout leaders need:
- Belief in youth and willingness to invest in them
- Real concern for young people's happiness and well-being
- Faith in Scouting as character-building program
- Understanding that Scouting is a game with a purpose
- Recognition that they represent Scouting to youth
- Steadfastness to carry out planned programs
- Willingness to let youth leaders lead
- Desire to advance through training opportunities
- Readiness to work with home, religious institutions, schools, and community
- Love of outdoors and vision of its educational value

### Servant Leadership

Scout leaders are servant leaders who put others' needs first and lead with empathy, community focus, and dedication to others' growth over personal recognition.

### Adult Leader Position Descriptions

**Scoutmaster:**
- Complete position-specific training including IOLS
- Ensure program achieves Scouting's aims using Scouting's methods  
- Train and guide youth leaders
- Work with senior patrol leader and attend patrol leaders' council meetings
- Coordinate assistant Scoutmasters
- Work with troop committee and report at meetings
- Ensure camping opportunities and advancement progress
- Conduct Scoutmaster conferences
- Ensure BSA policies are followed

**Assistant Scoutmaster positions** may include:
- Assistant Scoutmaster for Program
- Assistant Scoutmaster for New-Scout Patrol
- Assistant Scoutmaster for Older-Scout Patrol
- Assistant Scoutmaster for Leadership Development
- Assistant Scoutmaster for Traditional Patrols (one per patrol)

Each position has specific responsibilities supporting youth leadership and program delivery.

## Working With Parents

Parents play essential roles as supporters, helpers, and potential leaders. Successful Scoutmasters work with parents as partners.

### Parents As Parents

Set clear expectations for:
- Scout attendance standards and uniform policy
- Participation in money-earning projects and courts of honor
- Support of Scout advancement work
- Completion of health forms and resource surveys
- Communication about important issues affecting participation

Also clarify what parents should NOT do:
- Sign off on advancement unless trained and registered
- Do things their Scout can do independently
- Buy equipment without checking troop needs

### Communicating With Parents

Establish multiple communication methods beyond Scout-to-Scout communication:
- New parent conferences for all joining Scouts
- Problem-solving meetings when issues arise
- Meetings when Scouts take leadership positions
- Eagle Scout planning meetings

**New Parent Conference** should:
- Introduce parents to Scouts BSA program differences from Cub Scouting
- Show how program serves youth development
- Explain how specific troop operates
- Set expectations for parents and Scouts
- Show how parent participation helps Scouts succeed
- Identify volunteer opportunities

### Parents As Helpers

Involve parents in tasks that don't require adult leader training:
- Supply pickup and administrative tasks
- Phone calls and photocopying
- Money-earning project leadership
- Transportation and meal preparation
- Ceremony support and equipment maintenance

Use sign-up sheets at annual meetings and ensure parent helpers complete Youth Protection training.

### Parents As Potential Leaders

Evaluate parent helpers for leadership potential by observing:
- Good example setting
- Work quality with Scouts and adults
- Reliability and responsibility
- Alignment with Scouting values
- Time and talent availability

## Working With the Troop Committee

The troop committee functions as a board of directors while the Scoutmaster corps works directly with Scouts - like the difference between a school board and faculty.

### Understanding Roles and Responsibilities

**Troop Committee:** Sets policies and handles administration
**Scoutmaster Corps:** Implements policies and delivers program

This division provides advantages:
- Scoutmaster can focus on Scouts and program
- Board of review members are separate from direct leaders
- Committee provides clearer perspective on problems
- Allows focus on both day-to-day and long-term planning

### Working With Committee Counterparts

Adults with overlapping responsibilities must communicate and coordinate efforts. The Scoutmaster attends monthly committee meetings as a participating (but non-voting) member.

### Bridging Communication Gaps

Maintain communication through:
- Regular committee member visits to troop meetings
- Cross-training between committee and Scoutmaster corps
- Rotating people between committee and direct leadership roles
- Regular informal meetings between key leaders

## Adult Leader Training

Every Scout deserves a trained leader. Training should be viewed as continuous professional development, not a one-time requirement.

### The Adult Training Continuum

**Joining:** Youth Protection training required before membership approval

**Orientation:** Introduction to position and troop operations

**Basic Training:** Position-specific training required for all leaders
- Scoutmaster corps: Leader Position-Specific Training plus Introduction to Outdoor Leader Skills (IOLS)
- Committee members: Troop Committee Challenge (online or classroom)

**Supplemental Training:** Courses on specific topics including:
- Safe Swim Defense, Safety Afloat, Climb On Safely, Hazardous Weather
- Marksmanship training
- Roundtable monthly meetings
- University of Scouting events

**Advanced Training:**
- **Wood Badge:** Premier leadership development course over two weekends
- **Powder Horn:** High-adventure resource management course
- **Philmont Leadership Challenge:** Week-long conference at Philmont Scout Ranch

### Recertification Requirements

These certifications must be renewed:
- Youth Protection (every 2 years)
- Hazardous Weather, Safe Swim Defense, Safety Afloat, Chain Saw Safety (every 2 years)  
- Paddle Craft Safety, Aquatics Supervision (every 3 years)

### Training Recognition

- Basic training completion allows wearing Trained Leader emblem
- Advanced training brings additional recognition and awards
- Training truly is "the gift that keeps on giving"

## Advancement Overview

Earning badges is an outcome of Scouting, not its purpose. The purpose is developing character and leadership. Advancement is one of eight methods of Scouting.

### The Elements of the Advancement Program

**Ranks:** Scout, Tenderfoot, Second Class, First Class, Star, Life, Eagle Scout, Eagle Palms

**Merit Badges:** Approximately 130 available covering core Scouting skills, life skills, and career/hobby introductions

### Ranks

**Scout Rank:** Earned by completing basic requirements including Scout Oath, Scout Law, motto, and slogan

**Tenderfoot, Second Class, First Class:** Sequential ranks covering basic skills in camping, cooking, first aid, physical fitness, nature, and citizenship. BSA recommends achieving First Class within first 12-18 months.

**Star, Life, Eagle Scout:** More flexible requirements including merit badges, service hours, and leadership positions. Each Scout follows their own path.

**Eagle Palms:** Additional recognition for Eagle Scouts under 18 who earn additional merit badges and demonstrate continued leadership.

### Merit Badges

Scouts may begin earning merit badges immediately upon joining and may earn them in any order. Eagle-required merit badges have blue borders; all others have green borders.

### The Four Steps to Advancement

1. **The Scout Learns:** Through active troop program and instruction
2. **The Scout is Tested:** Demonstrates skills to qualified leaders
3. **The Scout is Reviewed:** Participates in board of review
4. **The Scout is Recognized:** Receives badge and formal recognition

#### The Scoutmaster Conference

Required for each rank. Private discussion between Scout and Scoutmaster about growth, goals, and understanding of Scouting ideals. Should last 10-15 minutes for ranks through Life Scout.

A Scout cannot "pass" or "fail" a Scoutmaster conference - they meet the requirement by participating.

#### Board of Review

After completing all rank requirements, Scout meets with 3-6 troop committee members. Purpose is to determine quality of experience and encourage continued advancement, not to retest skills.

Reviews should:
- Put Scout at ease with friendly atmosphere
- Ask open-ended questions about Scouting experiences
- Discuss application of Scout Oath and Law
- Last about 15 minutes for ranks through Life Scout

#### Recognition

Scouts should receive rank badges immediately after board of review approval. Courts of honor every 3-4 months provide formal recognition for multiple advancement achievements.

### Merit Badge Program

Merit badge counselors (separate from troop leaders) sign off on merit badges. The program provides:
- Opportunities to learn valuable skills
- Introduction to lifetime hobbies and careers
- Challenge to overcome obstacles
- Social skills through adult interaction

Sessions with merit badge counselors must follow Youth Protection guidelines with proper supervision.

## Other Awards

Beyond ranks and merit badges, numerous awards recognize special skills and accomplishments:

### Awards for Scouts
- Mile Swim BSA
- National Honor Patrol Award  
- National Outdoor Badges (hiking, camping, aquatics, riding, shooting)
- National Medal for Outdoor Achievement
- Nova and Supernova Awards (STEM)
- Totin' Chip and Fireman's Chit
- Den Chief Award
- Various aquatic sport awards
- Conservation and service awards

### Awards for Adults
- District Award of Merit
- Silver Beaver (council level)
- Silver Antelope (region level)  
- Silver Buffalo (national level)
- Unit Leader Award of Merit
- Scouter's Training Award
- Scoutmaster's Key

### Awards for Both Scouts and Adults
- BSA Lifeguard certification
- 50-Miler Award
- Conservation Good Turn Award
- Historic Trails Award
- International Spirit Award
- Lifesaving and Heroism Awards
- Religious Emblems
- Order of the Arrow membership

## Financing the Troop

Effective money-raising allows troops to buy equipment, travel to high-adventure bases, and ensure proper training.

### Planning a Budget

Four-step budget process:
1. Treasurer, Scoutmaster, and scribe prepare rough draft
2. Patrol leaders' council reviews and provides feedback
3. Troop committee approves budget
4. Present budget to Scouts and families

**Typical expenses:** Registration fees, charter fees, training, insurance, advancement, activities, summer camp, program materials, uniforms, reserve fund

**Typical income:** Weekly/monthly dues, annual membership fees, money-earning projects, chartered organization support

### Money-Earning Projects

Submit Unit Money-Earning Application to council before starting projects. Successful project ideas include:
- Collecting recyclables
- Product sales (excluding raffles/lotteries)
- Service provision (lawn care, car washing)
- Community meals
- Seasonal sales (Christmas trees, plants)
- Restaurant fundraising nights

### Evaluating Projects

Consider these questions:
- Do you really need the fundraiser?
- Are contracts signed by authorized individuals?
- Does project uphold BSA's good name?
- Will products be sold on their own merits?
- Does project avoid unethical activity?

### Supporting Scouts With Financial Needs

Ensure dues and fees don't create financial barriers. Many councils offer assistance funds, and troops should build aid into budgets. Payment of dues cannot be prerequisite for advancement.

The troop budget should balance the troop's needs with reasonable costs for families while maintaining adequate reserves for equipment and emergency expenses.

# Fundraising Guidelines

**Does the fundraising activity avoid competition with other units, the chartered organization, the local council, and United Way?** If the units with the chartered organization agree on the dates and type of project, this prevents unnecessary conflict.

**Does the fundraiser offer a reasonable return on your investment of time and effort?** For example, if you run a one-day carwash, you might need to raise only a few dollars while others spend entire weekends on a project.

Adult and youth members shall not be permitted to serve as directors of money-raising activities for Scouting or in support of other organizations. Adult and youth members shall not be permitted to conduct or to participate in events fund-raising activities sponsored by other organizations or international events.

## Encouraging Participation in Money-Earning Projects

Money-earning projects give Scouts and their families an opportunity to participate directly in fundraising. Consider setting up incentives. For instance, you could have a special recognition at the next outing for the patrol that sells the most popcorn.

## Chartered Organization Support

Chartered organizations have no obligation to provide financial support to the troop. Many do, however, which can strengthen the bond between the troop and the organization.

The primary support from the chartered organization is through providing a meeting place and maintaining that facility without charging dues or fees. Some chartered organizations provide financial support to help defray costs or help pay for camp.

---

# CHAPTER 22: Chartered Organization Relationships

Every Scout troop is chartered by a local religious institution, school, civic organization, or similar group. The group provides chartered organization status because it receives a charter from the Boy Scouts of America. This charter recognizes the organization's relationship to the troop, defines the responsibilities of both the organization and the BSA toward Scout leaders and the young people they serve.

The chartered organization can be an important source of support for the troop. You can picture the troop as a three-legged stool, with the Scoutmaster on one leg, the troop committee on another, and the chartered organization on the third leg. When each of these three legs is strong, the troop stands tall and strong.

## People and Purpose

Chartered organization relationships are strongest when people are committed and purposeful. When a chartered organization lacks this clarity, there will be confusion about what the organization does and how the troop and the organization work at cross purposes.

### Purpose

It is easy enough to figure out why the chartered organization exists. But why does it have a Scout troop? Once you understand that purpose, you can make sure you are helping the organization achieve it.

Assume for a moment that the troop belongs to a religious institution. If that institution sees Scouting as a way to serve existing member-families as part of its youth ministry, you would want to coordinate calendars with the youth director so activities don't conflict. If the institution sees Scouting primarily as an outreach tool, you might offer the religious leader ways to connect with troop families from the greater community through service projects and family campouts.

### People

The primary liaison between the troop and the organization is the chartered organization representative, that member of the organization chosen to maintain the relationship. That individual should be on the troop's mailing list and should be invited to troop committee meetings, courts of honor, banquets, and other high-profile functions. This person should also be consulted when problems arise that require chartered-organization involvement.

You may also find that other people want to stay in touch with the organization's executive officer such as its religious leader or president and extend invitations to events and the troop. Staying connected with these people gives you additional allies within the organization and makes it easier to coordinate activities.

From the troop side, it is usually helpful to have adult leaders (and troop families) who are members of the chartered organization. By their very presence, they emphasize that the troop belongs to the organization.

## Working Together

Once you know the organization, its people, and its purpose for operating a Scout troop, you can find many opportunities to work together. Here are a few things you could do:

- Ensure that the troop has a presence on the organization's website, in its membership materials, and on its bulletin board.
- Include chartered organization information in troop membership materials and on the troop website.
- Participate in the organization's annual kickoff events and open houses.
- Schedule regular updates or briefings for organization meetings—especially around Scouting Anniversary Week, the week that includes February 8 (Scouting's birthday).
- Participate in worship services during Scouting Anniversary Week.
- Invite organization leaders to attend a "state of the troop" report at an organization meeting.
- Set up recruiting tables at the organization to coordinate with School Night for Scouting or troop open houses.
- Set up tables at the organization to sell products during troop money-earning projects.
- Plan occasional service projects to benefit the chartered organization or to benefit other groups the chartered organization serves.
- Offer troop support of major chartered organization activities throughout the year.
- Look for opportunities for Scouts to do Eagle Scout service projects for the organization.

## National Chartered Organization Resources

Some chartered organizations that have a national presence and operate thousands of Scouting units have offices that are available to provide resources and support. The BSA's Committee on Scouting, for example, seeks to sustain and strengthen the relationship between the BSA and the Catholic Church in the United States, while in the United Methodist Church, Scouting falls under the Office of Youth-Serving Ministries.

Many national chartered organizations also have official or quasi-official websites and local councils or regional offices that support Scouting units.

The district executive is charged with visiting Scout troops chartered by the district's chartered organizations once a year. The district executive can be a good ally if you need to strengthen your troop's relationship with its chartered organization.

## Scout Sunday and Scout Sabbath

Scout Sunday and Scout Sabbath observed in Scout organizations worldwide—are an important way to celebrate and renew the chartered organization's relationship with Scouting. Scout Sunday is typically held on the Sunday closest to February 8 (Scouting's birthday), while Scout Sabbath is typically the Saturday that ends Scouting Anniversary Week.

There is no one way to hold a Scout Sunday or Scout Sabbath service. Ceremonies should be developed based on local time and culture, perhaps through the chartered organization representative, with Scouts reading, lighting a candle, delivering a benediction, or reading Scripture passages—if they are members of the chartered organization.

Examples include:
- Participation in the service by Scouts as ushers, readers, acolytes, speakers, or other roles
- Presentation of religious emblems to troop members and leaders of that faith
- Recognition of troop leaders
- A responsive reading that connects the points of the Scout Law with religious teachings
- A recognition of the religious heritage of Scouting values
- A Scouting appreciation booth
- A model campsite on the front lawn
- A pancake breakfast or chili luncheon that serves as a troop fundraiser

## BSA Adopt-a-School

The BSA Adopt-a-School program encourages Scouting units to select a school in their community. For each school selected, the troop completes at least one service project per quarter for the school over the course of a year. Scouts who participate in the service projects earn an Adopt-a-School patch. At the end of the year, the school receives a plaque.

---

# CHAPTER 23: Troop Communications

Americans today are awash in information. In fact, you could argue that it is now easier than ever to send a message and harder than ever to make sure your message gets received. That is why a Scoutmaster and troop committee must be every bit as strategic about getting the word out as they are in creating it.

Scout troops do not have marketing budgets—aside from the costs of photocopies, stamps, and website hosting—but they still must reach out to Scouts, leaders, parents, and other interested individuals and families.

## What to Communicate

When you think about troop communication, the first things that probably come to mind are upcoming activities and deadlines: What time will the court of honor start? Where will the campout be held? When are registration fees due? How much will summer camp cost?

This kind of information is usually shared through announcements and fliers at troop meetings or phone trees initiated by the senior patrol leader. However, an experienced Scoutmaster will appreciate that a message must reach both the Scout and the parents. That is why many troops publish newsletters with messages that reach both audiences.

**As a rule, people need to hear a message seven times in seven different ways before they will take action.**

You should not communicate solely about future events. It is also important to capture pictures and stories from past events. You can share these in troop newsletters, on the website, and include stories in the newsletter about those outings. Stories and pictures are great ways to sell the program to prospective members, to show the chartered organization what the troop is accomplishing, and to encourage current members to stay engaged with the troop program.

There is one more category of information you need to communicate: "evergreen" information about the troop that might appear in a brochure or troop handbook or on the troop website. New and prospective members need to know when the troop meets, where, and at what time; how to contact the leaders. Current members need to know how to sign up for boards of review, what rank they must be to run for senior patrol leader, where to buy uniforms and equipment, and how to access password-protected content on the troop website.

## With Whom to Communicate

Besides having different kinds of information to communicate, a troop has different groups of people with whom to communicate. The key audience, of course, is current troop families and leaders. However, you also need to keep the chartered organization, the schools where the troop recruits members, and the general public informed.

Different audiences obviously need to hear different messages. It is also important to safeguard certain information. Many troops have a two-tiered approach, with general information about the troop and contact information for key club leaders, and a password-protected members-only section with photo galleries and rosters.

## Communication Principles

The technologies you use to communicate will change over time, but basic principles stay the same:

**Be complete.** In your communications, try to answer the questions who, what, when, where, why and how. Rather than simply saying that there will be a campout in March, nail down that information first.

**Be clear.** Avoid jargon and unfamiliar abbreviations, especially with audiences beyond the troop. If information has changed since the last communication, clearly note the change and include revision dates on documents like troop calendars.

**Be consistent.** Send out communications like newsletters or emails on a regular schedule. For example, if you send your email newsletter by 5 p.m. on the day before every troop meeting, your Scouts and parents will come to expect it and plan accordingly.

**Don't overdo it.** Rather than send out half a dozen emails in a single week, bundle all those messages into one. Send a single packet with all troop information (medical forms, permission slips, activity signup forms, etc.) instead of handing out the documents separately.

**Use diverse media.** When you really need to get people's attention, send your message through multiple channels. A message about registration, posted through the troop calendar, the troop newsletter, fliers, a special mailing, a page on the troop website, a Facebook event, Twitter announcements, an article in the school newspaper, phone calls to Scouts and parents, and announcements at troop meetings will get noticed.

**Leverage the resources of the chartered organization.** Perhaps you'll have access to a Scout troop meeting room that also has a bulletin board, electronic sign boards, and other media.

**Understand people's communication preferences.** Find out how people want to get information. For many Scouts, email works fine, but for some parents, a phone call might work better. Don't assume; just ask.

## Unit Website Guidelines

Your unit website should be readily accessible. An attractive, well-maintained website is a great public relations tool that reaches people 24 hours a day, seven days a week. It can also be a good repository for information that parents and Scouts access frequently.

That said, you must make sure the website does not put Scouts at risk:

• The content of the site must be appropriate to Scouting.
• The site cannot link to any sites that contain material that is inappropriate to Scouting.
• The unit site should not contain any advertisements or promotional materials.
• The site cannot engage in the online sale of BSA merchandise or competing products.
• The site cannot replicate any BSA publication currently for sale by the Supply Division.
• Unit sites must consider the safety and privacy of members and participants by obtaining the necessary permissions to post photos and other personal information.
• Unit sites cannot engage in fundraising except as directly approved by and under the supervision of the local council.

## Social Media Guidelines

Social media such as Facebook, YouTube, and Twitter offer additional communication options and raise additional concerns. As with any Scouting activity, safety and BSA Youth Protection policies apply. Leaders should always use good judgment and make a commitment of the BSA to be an advocate for youth and to keep children and their privacy safe, both online and off.

To help ensure that all communication on social-media channels remains positive and safe, these channels must be public, and all leaders should follow a no-one-on-one rule. Private channels (e.g., private Facebook groups or invitation-only YouTube channels) are acceptable. Private channels and private messages should not be used. Remember, if you feel the information you seek to share via social-media channels should not be shared in public, do not share that information via social media.

Abiding by the two-deep leadership policy that governs all aspects of Scouting also applies to use of social media. As it relates to social media, two-deep leadership means there should be no private messages and no one-on-one direct contact between adult leaders and youth members on any platform (formerly Twitter, chats, instant messaging, Snapchat, etc.). All communication between adults and youth members should take place in a public forum. All private messages between adults and youth members should always include one or more authorized adults openly "copied" (included) on the message or thread.

If you set up a troop page on a site like Facebook or YouTube, you should designate at least two administrators who have access to the page, password, and page management information. At least one of these page administrators should be a registered leader with a current position number.

## Supporting Your Youth Leaders

Some Scouters feel that communication is such an important function that it can't be entrusted to youth leaders. While Scouts can certainly handle many communication tasks, adult leaders need to understand they can be closely involved. In fact, several positions of responsibility—scribe, historian, and webmaster—are directly related to communication. It is important to let youth leaders practice what they do.

Here are a few suggestions for getting Scouts more involved in communication:

• Set a troop standard that adult-driven communications only supplement, not replace, Scout-driven communications.
• Have the senior patrol leader, not an adult, make all announcements at troop meetings.
• Do not overlook the old-fashioned phone tree, where the senior patrol leader calls the patrol leaders each week and they call their Scouts.
• Encourage the historian to provide outing stories and pictures to the newsletter editor.
• Have the youth leaders in charge of outings create fliers for those outings.
• Set rewards for patrol newsletters or outing newsletters.

---

# APPENDIX

## Advancement Checklist

- What percentage of troop members advanced in the past year?
- What percentage of Life Scouts achieved the Eagle Scout rank in the past year?
- What percentage of new Scouts achieved First Class within 18 months?
- How many courts of honor does the troop hold each year?
- Do Scouts receive immediate recognition when they advance in rank? When they earn merit badges?
- Do meetings and outings offer advancement opportunities?

## Tips for Success

- Include immediate recognition during the closing of each meeting.
- Make advancement visible by using the Troop Advancement chart or other method.
- Hold regular Scoutmaster conferences for all Scouts, whether advancing or not.
- Offer merit badge sessions before meetings or at other times.
- If you use record-keeping software like ScoutBook, use the report function to identify Scouts who are not advancing or to identify advancement needs several Scouts share.
- Each month, highlight a different type of award (religious emblems, STEM awards, aquatic awards like Mile Swim BSA, etc.)

## Sources for Guest Instructors

- Council or district merit badge counselor directory
- Venturing crews that specialize in outdoor adventure
- Neighboring troops with expertise in a particular topic
- Camp staff members in the offseason
- Employees of local outdoor outfitters, bike shops, etc.
- Members of organizations like the Audubon Society or Orienteering USA
- Instructors with organizations like the American Red Cross or the American Canoe Association
- High school teachers, college faculty members, and graduate students
- Members of the chartered organization

## Preparing for the Annual Planning Conference

### Participants
- Committee Chair
- Scoutmaster
- Senior patrol leader

### Step 1: Gather key information.
- School dates (holidays, exams, etc.)
- Community event dates
- Chartered organization dates
- Personal dates
- District and council dates
- Troop Resource Survey data
- Last year's troop annual plan
- Troop priorities and goals
- Scouts' advancement records
- General outline of next year's program
- Dates already committed to (scheduled Philmont trek, national Scout jamborees, etc.)

### Step 2: Involve the senior patrol leader.
- Explain importance of process and the senior patrol leader's role.
- Discuss program and activity options.
- Discuss troop goals.
- Share outline of next year's program.
- Ask for the senior patrol leader's input and thoughts.
- Be flexible.

### Step 3: Gather input from Scouts.
- Senior patrol leader shares draft plan with patrol leaders.
- Patrol leaders share plan with Scouts.
- Patrol leaders schedule a meeting to get Scouts' feedback.
- Compare the current plan with the draft plan.

### Step 4: Invite people to attend the conference.
- Troop youth leaders
- Troop committee members
- Other adult leaders
- Chartered organization representative
- Your unit commissioner (optional)
- Anyone who might be helpful

## Holding the Annual Planning Conference—Checklist

### Part 1: Preparation
- Where should you hold the annual conference?
- Who should play an active role in it?
- What role should most adults play?
- What supplies will you need in the way of planners and calendars?
- Do you have a copy of the Troop Program Planning Chart?
- What should be the outcome of your conference?
- What program features do you want to include?
- What timetable have you established for your troop goals?
- How many traditional outings have you included on your calendar?
- Do you have a mix of familiar and unfamiliar activities?
- What are your conference ground rules?

### Part 2: The Conference, Step by Step
- **Step 1:** Lead a discussion on troop goals and arrive at an agreed list.
- **Step 2:** Share the draft calendar, review dates, and select additional ones.
- **Step 3:** Discuss and then vote to approve dates and events.
- **Step 4:** Invite the senior patrol leader to share updates from patrol leaders about programs and themes.
- **Step 5:** Add other important dates.
- **Step 6:** Hold final discussion and vote on plan, calendar, and goals.
- **Step 7:** Share the plan with each troop family.

## Patrol Leaders' Council Meeting—Checklist

- What is the purpose of the patrol leaders' council meeting?
- How many months of programming does the meeting cover?
- Why is delegating part of the planning so important for a successful PLC meeting?
- What are the advantages of automating some planning details?
- What are the rotating duty roster tasks that can be determined ahead of time?
- Describe the Scoutmaster's role before and during the PLC meeting.
- Coach the senior patrol leader on the agenda ahead of time.
- Be familiar with the troop's annual program plan.
- Provide resources.
- Be alert for plans the troop makes that call for special resources or support.
- Help the senior patrol leader keep the meeting on track.
- Share a Scoutmaster's Minute or training module.
- To whom should the Scoutmaster distribute the monthly plan after the meeting?

## The Purposes of Troop Meetings—Checklist

- Prepare for outings.
- Learn and practice Scouting skills.
- Exercise leadership.
- Strengthen patrols.
- Promote advancement and personal growth.
- Inspire Scouts.
- Have fun.

### You know the troop meeting is a success when each troop member…
- Learns a new skill
- Completes an advancement requirement
- Prepares for an upcoming outing without parental assistance
- Enjoys fellowship with other Scouts and leaders
- Accomplishes something as a leader

## Using Adult Leaders at Scout Meetings—Checklist

### Designated adult leaders can...
- Conduct opening and closing ceremonies.
- Recognize Scouts who have advanced.
- Teach skills at the meeting, and quietly remind the senior patrol leader when it goes off schedule.
- Touch base with youth leaders running part of the meeting to see if they are prepared.
- Sit in on patrol meetings and skills instruction to provide quiet support to the youth leaders.
- Work with their youth leader counterparts.
- Conduct Scoutmaster conferences and boards of review.
- Serve as a merit badge counselor, and sign off on merit badge requirements.
- Watch for Scouts who are not involved or who wander off.

### Adult leaders should never...
- Take over the meeting (unless it is a true emergency).
- Criticize or call out Scouts.
- Yell "Quiet!" (Controlling the group is the senior patrol leader's responsibility, and the Scout sign is a silent sign!)

### Tips for Success
- Contact individuals contributing to the event beforehand to confirm their participation.
- Ensure that senior patrol leader and assistant senior patrol leader arrive early for a quick briefing.
- Plan your Scoutmaster's Minutes well.
- Provide variety, action, and purpose.
- Move the meeting outdoors when possible.
- Encourage youth leaders to use the Scout sign to get and hold attention.
- Do not over-organize; alternate a favorite game or activity with others.
- Mix up your games alternating between skill, speed, dexterity, and wits.
- Always leave the Scouts wanting more.
- Start and end on time.

## Planning Patrol Activities—Frequently Asked Questions

**Q. When can a patrol day hike or service project without adult supervision be allowed?**

**A:** Never. Two registered adult leaders 21 years of age or over are required at all Scouting activities, including all meetings. There must be a registered female adult leader or another adult female age 18 or over for every five female youth participants (chartered organization families). A registered female adult leader 21 years of age or over must be present for any activity involving female youth or female adult program participants.

**Q. What should a Scoutmaster consider before allowing a patrol day hike or service project?**

**A:** The Scoutmaster should be satisfied that the patrol is mature, thoroughly planned and that it is well within each patrol member's level of training and responsibility. The patrol activity cannot interfere with any troop function.

**Q. What should the Scoutmaster do if there are any doubts?**

**A:** The Scoutmaster should encourage the patrol to include an adult on the plans.

**Q. What are the requirements for overnight patrol activities?**

**A:** Two registered adult leaders 21 years of age or over are required at all Scouting activities, including all meetings. There must be a registered female adult leader or another adult female age 18 or over for every five female youth participants (chartered organization families). A registered female adult leader 21 years of age or over must be present for any activity involving female youth or female adult program participants.

All adults staying overnight in connection with a Scouting activity must be currently registered as an adult volunteer or an adult program participant.

## Planning Outings—Checklist

### Basic Planning Details
- Begin planning as far back as the annual planning conference.
- Have key details like location, cost, and departure finalized at the previous month's PLC meeting.
- Have Scouts sign up a week or two in advance to allow time to plan for food and have drivers and adult leaders in place.
- Have youth leaders set the agenda and plan the outing process.
- If expenses are involved, have the Scouts develop the basic costs for the outing so they learn budgeting for the event.

### Choosing Campsites
- To find a good campsite, use your contacts, experience, and resources.
- Look for campsites where each patrol can have its own clearly defined space.
- Have adults camp at a convenient distance to allow for youth independence and development.
- Contact the campsite owner or manager well in advance and keep that relationship.
- Pay attention to the landowner's or land manager's regulations and possible limitations.
- Ensure that Scouts and adults act responsibly and in keeping with Leave No Trace and Tread Lightly! guidelines, even in developed campsites.

### Program Activities and Schedules
- Have the Scouts select activities that tie to the troop's focus, whether it is hiking, special cooking, wilderness survival, etc.
- Have Scouts secure equipment as needed and recruit consultants as needed for planned activities.
- Create an expectation for starting and ending on time.
- Be sure to allow time in the schedule for unexpected opportunities that arise along the way.
- Don't be so rigid with the schedule that there is no time for unstructured play and free time.
- Be prepared with rainy-day activities.

### Menus and Shopping
- Have each patrol plan its menu, estimate the cost, buy the ingredients, and do the cooking with adult supervision for the outing.
- Have the patrol quartermaster or a Scout working on a cooking-related advancement help with meal planning and the shopping.
- Create menus depending on the type of outing—limit backpacking patrols to lightweight foods, for example, or create more elaborate menus for base camp outings.
- Discourage parents, who typically help Scouts do the shopping, from going beyond the planned menu.

### Tips for Success
- Keep in mind that as of Jan. 1, 2014, the Cooking merit badge is required for the Eagle Scout rank.
- Add variety to menus by using themes or creating cooking contests.
- For Scouts who are learning to create a menu, consider taking them to a grocery store.

### Duty Rosters
- Make a list of all the chores involved in cooking a meal or in patrol camping that make it a collaborative effort.
- For younger Scouts, you may need to spell out clearly who does what and when by creating a duty roster.
- Create a chart that lists each meal vertically down the left side and each chore horizontally across the top. Chores include shopping, setup, cooking, and cleanup.
- Rotate who performs each chore from meal to meal to create a sense of fairness.
- Remember: cleanup is everyone's responsibility; have each patrol leader lead the patrol in making a thorough sweep of the campsite and leaving it better than they found it.

### Using Adult Leaders on Outings
- Ask the Scouts to set up camp a short distance from the adults' camp to make the outings a place where mentoring relationships are strengthened and Scouts can feel more ownership with these trusted adults.
- Conduct youth-leader conferences for advancement or for Scouts who should be advancing.
- Review the BSA Youth Protection guidelines, which require that one adult always be within sight and hearing of others.
- Make sure tents are pitched properly.
- Ensure that Scout beds will keep Scouts comfortable, warm, and dry.
- Encourage Scouts to change from sweat-soaked clothing to dry sleepwear.
- If hot weather, make sure Scouts use sunscreen and drink plenty of water.
- Plan to monitor their urine output to avoid dehydration. It should be clear and copious.
- Watch out for signs of homesickness, health problems, or discipline issues.
- Help the patrol leader and senior patrol leader enforce bedtimes so that everyone gets enough sleep.
- Typically, adults go to bed last and get up first.

### Outdoor Equipment—Checklist
- Know the requirements for the specific type of outing you are planning.
- Promote the use of lightweight camping gear to make it easier for Scouts to carry.
- Suggest that each Scout carry the Scout Basic Essentials as outlined in the Scouts BSA Handbook.
- Keep in mind that using cell phones and other electronic devices on outings can be a contentious issue; many such devices have useful features.
- With the input of the patrol leaders' council, guide your troop in developing its own policies about when to allow and when to restrict them.

## Scout Basic Essentials—Checklist

- Pocketknife or multi-tool
- First-aid kit
- Extra clothing
- Rain gear
- Water bottle (and method for treating water in the backcountry)
- Flashlight
- Trail food
- Matches and fire starters
- Sun protection
- Map and compass

*Insect repellent, a whistle, and other items also might be considered essentials, depending on your destination, the length of your trip, and the season.*

## Personal Overnight Camping Gear—Checklist

### Scout Basic Essentials
- Clothing appropriate for the season
- Backpack with rain cover
- Sleeping bag or two or three blankets
- Sleeping pad
- Ground cloth
- Eating kit
  - Spoon
  - Plate
  - Bowl
  - Cup
- Cleanup kit
  - Soap
  - Toothbrush
  - Dental floss
  - Comb
  - Washcloth
  - Towel
  - Hand sanitizer

### Additional Items
- Plastic garbage bags
- Nylon cord, 50 feet
- Optional personal extras and gear for specific activities
  - Watch
  - Camera
  - Small notebook
  - Pencil or pen
  - Sunglasses
  - Small musical instrument
  - Swimsuit
  - Gloves
  - Fishing pole and gear

## Troop and Patrol Gear—Checklist

### Before Purchasing
Before purchasing a new piece of equipment, be sure that:
- It fits a definite need.
- It enhances the troop's program.
- There is space to store it.
- It is in good condition and safe.
- There are no hidden costs.
- You can afford it.

### Troop Overnight Camping Gear
- Tents with poles, stakes, ground cloths, and lines
- Dining fly
- Nylon cord, 50 feet
- Backpacking stoves and fuel
- Group first-aid kit
- Washbasin
- Cook kit—one per patrol and one for the adults
- Cleanup kit—one per patrol and one for the adults
- Repair kit (thread, needles, safety pins)

### Troop Extras (optional)
- Hot-pot tongs
- Camp shovel
- Water container—one 5-gallon or two 1-gallon collapsible, plastic
- Grill
- Pot hooks
- Patrol flag
- Small U.S. flag
- Ax
- Camp saw
- Lanterns and fuel

## Storing and Caring for Troop Equipment—Checklist

### Follow commonsense guidelines:
- Write, sketch, or engrave identifying information
- Store equipment in a locked space that is temperature and humidity controlled
- Have the troop quartermaster work with a member of the troop committee to organize the gear and set up an efficient check-out and check-in system
- Check the condition of each item after use, and arrange for any cleaning or repair
- Maintain a list of the gear on hand

### Tents and Tarps
- Two-person tents are ideal for most patrol and troop camping because they can be carried in backpacks, and their use fosters the buddy system
- Use a tarp or dining fly to fend off the weather or to protect gear stored underneath

### Trailers
Keep these ideas in mind regarding trailers:
- Be aware of its towing requirements when purchasing a trailer
- Unload the trailer completely after each outing to ensure that everything gets packed on the next outing
- Check to be sure your trailer; some campsites can only be accessed on foot
- Protect your trailer by buying and using a wheel lock
- Add padlocks to all the doors
- Park your trailer in a highly visible location in such a way that it can't be easily moved

## Scoutmaster's Campsite Quick Checklist

### While Setting Up Camp
- Choose a campsite according to Leave No Trace and Tread Lightly! guidelines
- Determine the kitchen area and tent locations before setting up camp

### Tents
- Inspect tents to keep out wind and rain
- Tie guylines with correct knots; properly stake them down
- Use ground cloths under tents to shield tent floors from water and ground moisture

### Personal Gear and Bedding
- Stow sleeping bags and pads inside tents
- Store clothing and other personal gear neatly in packs

### Kitchen Area
- Set up the dining fly
- Stow food to keep it safe from weather and wildlife
- Properly set up the cooking area, stoves, and campfires using Leave No Trace and Tread Lightly! guidelines
- Plan and follow plans for wastewater disposal and trash management
- Set out soap and water for Scouts to wash hands before cooking and eating

### Latrine
- Arrange for cleaning of outhouses and monitoring of toilet facilities (if available)
- Be sure the general locations of catholes if used are explained to all Scouts
- Be sure every Scout has been trained to use catholes and latrines in an environmentally sound manner
- Keep toilet paper on hand

## During the Campout

### Campsite
- Keep the area clean and orderly.
- Pick up all litter, even debris left by other groups.

### Tents
- Keep tents taut.
- Neatly stow bedding and personal gear.

### Kitchen Area
- Properly store all food.
- Keep utensils and dishes clean and orderly.
- Keep cooking and dining areas neat.
- Have scouts follow duty rosters for meal preparation and cleanup.
- Be sure Scouts wash their hands with soap and water before cooking and eating.
- Dispose of dishwater and manage trash and leftovers according to Leave No Trace and Tread Lightly! guidelines.

### Scouts
- Dress appropriately for the weather and the activities.
- Follow personal cleanliness habits (within reason).
- Be sure the general health of all Scouts is good.

### While Breaking Camp
- Check to make sure personal and group gear has been cleaned and is ready for the trip home.
- Pick out all trash for transport and proper disposal.
- Check that all catholes have been properly covered. Properly dispose of all toilet paper.
- Make sure all tents, tarps, and other items have been put away clean and inspected have been cleaned of any litter and bits of leftover food.
- Where appropriate, as a courtesy for the next campers, leave a supply of firewood.
- Look over the campsite again from a Leave No Trace and Tread Lightly! point of view. Note any other Scouts can take to improve conditions in the area.

## Outing Planner

| Description |  |
| Location |  |
| Departure date and time |  |
| Return date and time |  |
| Youth leader in charge of planning |  |
| Adult leader in charge of planning |  |
| Expected attendance (Scouts) |  |
| Expected attendance (adults) |  |

### Three Months Out
- Assign youth and adult coordinators.
- Confirm dates and times.
- Reserve campsite.
- Recruit program specialists (e.g., certified COPE instructor).

### Two Months Out
- Plan the budget; set the price.
- Secure needed permits (e.g., campsite reservation, fishing and boating licenses).
- Begin promotion.
- Plan special training or shakedowns, including troop training events.
- Begin issuing and distributing permission slips, including those required by outfitters.
- Begin recruiting adult leaders and drivers.

### One Month Out
- Include a schedule.
- Collect any missing Annual Health and Medical Record forms.
- Begin teaching needed skills at troop meetings.
- Ensure at least one leader has training in basic first aid and wilderness first aid for backcountry trips, hazardous weather preparedness, Mental Health First Aid, Wilderness First Responder, Leave No Trace, Winter Camping, Wilderness Survival, Orienteering, Wilderness Fire Safety, Safe Swim Defense, Safety Afloat, and/or Climb On Safety.
- Continue promotion.
- Secure any special troop and patrol equipment (e.g., canoes, climbing gear).

### Two Weeks Out
- Use your two-week phone call to ensure drivers and enough gear haulers.
- Check the route and prepare maps as needed.
- Confirm the location of rest stops on long trips. (Scouts should not travel in tight convoys.)
- Confirm the nearest medical facility and emergency phone number.
- Confirm with a parent and phone number.
- Prepare daily activities.

### One Week Out
- Confirm Scout and adult attendance.
- Collect permission slips.
- Confirm the campsite reservation.
- Confirm the participation of program specialists.
- Have patrols plan menus, assign shopping duties, and prepare duty rosters.
- Assemble troop and patrol equipment.
- Do a personal gear shakedown, if necessary.
- Check the weather forecast.
- Announce the departure and arrival times, if not already announced.

### Day of Outing
- Do a final gear and food check.
- Distribute emergency contact information to parents.
- Share travel plans with someone who is staying behind.
- Leave on time, have fun, and be safe.
