## Glossary

**board of review.** A review held to determine whether a Scout has satisfactorily completed rank requirements. A review may also be held to encourage Scouts who are not advancing.

**campmaster.** A volunteer Scouter trained in short-term camping.

**charter.** In the BSA, officers authorize (1) an organization to serve as a chartered organization for a BSA local council; (2) operation of an Order of the Arrow lodge; or (3) the Boy Scouts of America to incorporate.

**charter presentation.** A formal ceremony at which the charter, Scout organization and membership certificates are presented to organization authorities and members of the unit.

**charter renewal.** An annual meeting attended by the chartered organization's representatives, troop leaders, and unit commissioner for the purpose of reviewing the charter application and making plans for the continued operation of the unit.

**chartered organization.** A religious, civic, fraternal, educational, or community-based organization that has applied for and received a charter to operate a BSA Scouting unit.

**chartered organization representative.** A manager of Scouting in a chartered organization who also represents the organization in the local council and district.

**commissioner.** A commissioned Scouter who works with Cub Scout packs, Scout troops, and Venturing crews to help them achieve excellence.

**council service center.** The business center for the local administration of Scouting.

**council.** An administrative body chartered to be responsible for Scouting in a designated geographic territory.

**court of honor.** A recognition ceremony for those who have met the requirements of any of the Scouts BSA ranks, merit badges, or other awards.

**district.** A geographical area of the council determined by the council executive board to help ensure the growth and success of Scouting units within the district's territory.

**district executive.** A professional Scouter who works under the direction of the council and serves as an advisor to the volunteer leaders in the district.

**Friends of Scouting.** An annual opportunity for Scouters and interested people in the community to be identified with the local council through their financial support and influence in the community and the program.

**Good Turn.** A distinctive feature of Scouts BSA is its emphasis on community service. It is a Good Turn when help is given that all Scouts endeavor to secure.

**Jamboree.** A national or international gathering of Scouts.

**Journey to Excellence.** A performance recognition program designed to encourage and reward success and measure the performance of units, districts, and councils.

**new-Scout Scoutmaster.** A 16- or 17-year-old trained youth member who is assigned and responsible to the Scoutmaster for tasks assigned.

**Leave No Trace.** A nationally recognized outdoor skills and ethics awareness organization that helps to reduce impacts on the environment and other people. Its mission informs the BSA's outdoor program.

**local council.** An administrative body chartered by the National Council. It is responsible for Scouting in a designated geographic territory.

**local council service center.** The business center for the local administration of Scouting.

**lodge.** A local council Order of the Arrow group chartered annually by the National Council.

**My.Scouting.** An internet portal for our members that provides records management, unit tools, E-Learning Course Management System, and more.

**new-Scout patrol.** Upon joining a Scout troop, a new youth member may become part of a patrol composed of new Scouts, where an assistant Scoutmaster and a troop guide help ensure a smooth transition to troop life.

**older-Scout patrol.** A patrol of Scouts ages 13 and older in a troop. The patrol participates in high-adventure activities.

**Order of the Arrow.** Scouting's national honor society. The aim of the OA is to promote the outdoor program and service to Scouting.

**outdoor ethics.** A set of principles that guide Scouts' ethical decision making in their relationship to the natural world.

**patrol.** A small group of Scouts usually five to eight who belong to a troop. Patrols have their own identities, uniforms, and meetings. Normally, there are several patrols in one troop.

**patrol leaders' council.** Each patrol leader, representing the patrol, meets with other patrol leaders and the senior patrol leader to plan their troop program. The Scoutmaster acts as an advisor.

**roundtable.** An event conducted by a roundtable commissioner and roundtable staff to help the unit leaders of a district plan and carry out their unit programs.

**Scout benediction.** "May the Great Scoutmaster of all Scouts be with us until we meet again."

**Scout executive.** The chief executive officer of the local council responsible for the administration, financing, marketing, membership, program, and delivery of all council-sponsored and council operations.

**Scout Life.** The magazine for all Scouts, published by the Boy Scouts of America.

**Scout Sabbath.** The Saturday after February 8, Scouting Anniversary Day.

**Scout shop.** A BSA-owned store, operated by the local council, that sells official Scouting merchandise.

**Scout Sunday.** The Sunday before February 8, Scouting Anniversary Day.

**Scouter.** An adult leader in the Boy Scouts of America.

**Scouting Anniversary Week.** The week that includes February 8, Scouting Anniversary Day. During this week, units are encouraged to conduct rededication ceremonies and to demonstrate Scouting's purposeful activities.

**Scouting magazine.** The official digital magazine online and in print for adults involved in Scouting to inspire and strengthen a desire to serve.

**Scoutmaster.** A volunteer Scouter 21 or older, appointed by the chartered organization to lead a Scout troop.

**Scoutmaster's Minute.** A part of the closing ceremony of a troop meeting or campfire in which the Scoutmaster encourages Scouts by telling a story.

**Sea Scouting.** A branch of Venturing that specializes in water-based activities, e.g., sailing, motorboating, and maritime careers.

**senior patrol leader.** A Scout elected by the Scouts to help all the patrols succeed. Each troop has one senior patrol leader who may be assisted by one or more assistant senior patrol leaders.

**service center.** See local council service center.

**square knot.** Generally, embroidered square knots are representative of special awards and pin on neckerchief awards.

**Supply Group.** The arm of the Boy Scouts of America that supplies official uniforms, equipment, literature, and other resources to the field. Includes administrative offices located in the national office, the National Distribution Center, and Scout shops through council resources.

**trading post.** The camp or reservation store where campers may purchase equipment and supplies. A distributor's Scouting department is sometimes referred to as the trading post.

**Tread Lightly!** A national nonprofit organization with a mission to promote responsible outdoor recreation through ethics education to protect our natural resources and outdoor ethics principles.

**troop.** The entity that conducts the Scouts BSA program for the chartered organization. It is typically composed of several patrols.

**unit.** The entity that conducts Scouting for the chartered organization. It consists of registered youth members and registered adult volunteer members. A unit may be a Cub Scout pack, Boy Scout troop, Venturing crew, or Sea Scout ship. Units are administered by the unit committee, which is appointed by the chartered organization.

**Venturing.** The young adult program of the Boy Scouts of America for men and women ages 14 through 20, or 13 with parental consent, to engage in exciting outdoor activities.

**Youth Protection.** The BSA emphasizes fights child abuse by teaching youth the "three R's": recognize, resist, and report child abuse. Troops are provided information and tools to help Scouts recognize potential abuse and leaders learn to teach youth how to report abuse situations or reports.

---

# Troop Leader Guidebook Vol 2

# Scouts BSA Troop Leader Guidebook Volume 2

## SECTION 1: ASSESSMENT AND IMPROVEMENT

### CHAPTER 1: Troop Vision

The mission of Scouting America is to prepare young people to make ethical and moral choices over their lifetimes by instilling in them the values of the Scout Oath and Scout Law.

Scouting America's vision is for every young person to become a responsible, participating citizen and leader guided by the Scout Oath and Scout Law.

#### Criteria for a Meaningful Vision

- A vision engages the heart and spirit
- A vision aims toward a worthwhile goal
- A vision gives meaning to an effort
- A vision is simple
- A vision is attainable
- A vision can change over time

### CHAPTER 2: Evaluating Your Troop

## Journey to Excellence

Journey to Excellence (JTE) uses a balanced scorecard approach to help leaders assess troop performance across 11 areas:

- Planning and budget
- Building membership
- Retention
- Advancement
- Arrow of Light-to-Scout transition
- Training
- Outdoor adventure
- Long-term camping
- Service projects
- Leadership
- Family engagement
- Trained leadership

### JTE Status Levels

- **Bronze:** Earn at least 525 points by earning points in at least 8 objectives (satisfactory Scouting)
- **Silver:** Earn at least 750 points by earning points in at least 8 objectives (excellent Scouting)  
- **Gold:** Earn at least 1,000 points by earning points in at least 8 objectives (outstanding Scouting)

## Troop Self-Assessment

For each question, indicate whether you strongly disagree (0), neither agree nor disagree (1), or strongly agree (2).

### Membership
1. The troop is growing in size
2. The troop retains at least 75 percent of its members each year, not counting those who age out or transfer
3. The troop has a membership growth plan that includes at least one annual recruiting event
4. The troop maintains a strong, year-round relationship with at least one feeder pack
5. The troop regularly recruits new adult leaders
6. The troop has a designated, active Webelos-to-Scout coordinator
7. The troop has an established system for smoothly integrating new Scouts

### Troop Meetings
8. Each troop meeting is fun and has a purpose
9. Meeting space is adequate for current and future needs
10. Troop meetings follow the patrol method
11. Troop meetings build toward upcoming outings
12. Troop meetings include activities that encourage physical fitness
13. Troop meetings are Scout-led with minimal direct adult involvement
14. Attendance at troop meetings is good
15. Meetings start and end on time
16. The patrol leaders' council meets before and after troop meetings

### Activities/Outings
17. The troop maintains a calendar of monthly outings, most of them overnight
18. The troop holds a variety of activities at a mix of traditional and new destinations
19. The troop attends a long-term summer camp each year
20. The troop plans adventure outings at least once every two years
21. Each outing offers something of interest to Scouts of all ages
22. Each outing provides opportunities for advancement, skill development, the patrol method, and leadership
23. Youth leaders play a key role in planning and leading all outings
24. The troop plans at least five service projects per year, including at least one that benefits the chartered organization

### Advancement
25. 80% of Scouts are advancing at least one rank per year
26. Scouts have opportunities to earn merit badges beyond summer camp
27. Troop leaders make it easy for Scouts to find merit badge counselors
28. The troop regularly highlights both Eagle-required and elective merit badges
29. Scouts regularly receive updated advancement reports
30. Scoutmaster conferences and boards of review are scheduled promptly when requested
31. The troop has a board of review for Scouts who are not advancing
32. The troop tracks Scouts who are within a year of turning 18 and encourages them to work toward the Eagle Scout rank
33. Scouts receive immediate recognition of their achievements at troop meetings
34. Inspiring courts of honor are held three times a year and are well attended by both Scouts and their families

### Patrols
35. The troop has functioning patrols with names, patrol patches, and other indicators of their identity
36. Patrols are large enough to function effectively even if one or two members are absent
37. Scouts have the opportunity to choose their own patrols
38. All Scouts know the patrol they are in and their patrol leader
39. Individual Scouts within each patrol have patrol-level positions of responsibility
40. Patrol leaders are in regular contact with their patrol members outside of troop activities

### Youth Leaders
41. The troop holds elections for patrol leaders and senior patrol leader at least once a year
42. Youth leaders have regular meetings with the Scoutmaster
43. Newly elected or appointed leaders complete Introduction to Leadership Skills for Troops within a few weeks of taking office
44. Most troop-level youth leaders have attended National Youth Leadership Training
45. The troop holds monthly patrol leaders' council meetings
46. Patrol leaders' council meetings are run by the senior patrol leader with minimal Scoutmaster intervention
47. Attendance at patrol leaders' council meetings is good
48. Patrol leaders' council meetings start and end on time
49. Patrol leaders' council meetings include training components and opportunities to discuss issues facing the troop
50. The troop scribe records and distributes minutes of patrol leaders' council meetings

### Scoutmaster Corps
51. The troop regularly recruits new assistant Scoutmasters
52. Assistant Scoutmasters have assigned areas of responsibility
53. The Scoutmaster coaches Scouts on issues and challenges
54. The Scoutmaster and all assistant Scoutmasters have completed basic training; some have completed Wood Badge
55. Enough leaders have completed specialized training (Safe Swim Defense, Climb On Safely, CPR, First Aid) so that the Scouts are not held back from pursuing activities
56. The troop has sufficient leadership depth so that no adult leader is overextended or unsupported
57. The troop has enough adult leaders so that no troop activity is canceled due to a lack of sufficient adult leadership
58. An annual leadership plan is in place for the Scoutmaster and key assistant Scoutmasters

### Troop Committee
59. The troop committee provides the Scoutmaster with full support
60. The troop committee handles administrative details, freeing the Scoutmaster corps to focus on program
61. The troop committee meets monthly
62. Troop committee members have assigned areas of responsibility
63. Troop committee members have completed basic training; some have completed Wood Badge
64. The troop committee chair attends monthly troop meetings and provides a report
65. The Scoutmaster attends monthly troop committee meetings and provides a report
66. The troop committee chair runs troop committee meetings and serves as an active liaison with the chartered organization
67. The Scoutmaster meets with the committee chair monthly
68. The troop committee has a succession plan in place for the committee chair, key committee members, and the Scoutmaster

### Miscellaneous
69. The troop has a written budget that is reviewed at troop committee meetings and that is sufficient for troop operations
70. The troop budget includes funds to provide financial support to needy Scouts and to subsidize leader training costs
71. The troop conducts at least one money-earning project per year to pay for activities, equipment, training, and other expenses
72. All money-earning projects are sufficiently brief so as to not detract from troop programming
73. Both Scouts and adult leaders wear correct Scout uniforms to troop meetings, courts of honor, and other events as appropriate
74. The troop has multiple points of contact with the chartered organization (participation in Scout Sunday or Scout Sabbath, overlap in membership, joint service projects, etc.)
75. The troop completes the charter renewal process on time each year
76. The troop regularly receives Journey to Excellence recognition

## STOP Technique

When lost during navigation or troop operations:

**S** – Stop where you are
**T** – Think about how you got to this position and what you know about getting to where you want to go
**O** – Observe what is around you and take advantage of the ideas of those who are with you
**P** – Plan a coherent course of action to get you to the next place on your journey

### CHAPTER 3: Evaluating Your Leadership Team

The Key 3—Scoutmaster, committee chair, and chartered organization representative—should conduct an annual confidential evaluation of the troop's leadership team using a SWOT analysis:

- **Strengths:** Eagle Scout; Wood Badge-trained; excellent rapport with Scouts
- **Weaknesses:** Shift job makes regular attendance difficult; works better with adults than Scouts
- **Opportunities:** Plans to attend Powder Horn; could help build high-adventure program
- **Threats:** Child is close to aging out; health issues make participation difficult

## Terms of Service and Term Limits

Rather than allowing leaders to continue indefinitely, some troops recruit for one year at a time and reassess assignments annually. Some troops set term limits on key positions like Scoutmaster to ensure fresh energy.

### CHAPTER 4: Evaluating Yourself

## A Self-Assessment Exercise

Five questions to consider:

1. Why did you become a troop leader?
2. Why do you want to be a leader now?
3. What does Scouting America expect of you?
4. What do you expect of Scouting?
5. What do you expect of yourself?

## 360-Degree Assessment

Feedback comes from supervisors, peers, subordinates, and sometimes customers. In a troop setting, this might include feedback from Scouts, parents, fellow leaders, and other adults.

### Tips on Giving Feedback

1. Listen carefully, then rephrase what you hear
2. Deal only with behavior that can be changed
3. Deal with specifics, not generalities
4. Describe the behavior; do not evaluate it
5. Let the person know the impact the behavior had
6. Use "I" statements to accept responsibility for your perceptions
7. Ask recipients to rephrase what you said to verify understanding

### Tips on Receiving Feedback

1. Seek out feedback
2. Listen carefully
3. Listen actively—rephrase feedback in your own words
4. Listen nonjudgmentally
5. Note your emotional reactions but don't let them cloud your ability to listen

### CHAPTER 5: Charting a Course

## Setting Priorities

Divide your list of issues into categories:
- **A priorities:** Things you must do
- **B priorities:** Things you should do  
- **C priorities:** Things that would be nice to do

Sort A priorities by importance: A-1, A-2, A-3, etc.

## Five Stages of Team-Based Project Planning

1. **Project Overview:** Summarize specific goals and objectives
2. **Work Breakdown Structure:** Identify tasks and order of completion
3. **Activity Assignments:** Assign responsibilities to team members
4. **Implementation:** Put the plan into action and track progress
5. **Closeout Report:** Compare original goals to final outcomes

### Project Goals and Objectives (SMART)

- **S**—Specific: Is it specific in targeting an objective?
- **M**—Measurable: What are the measurable indicators of progress or success?
- **A**—Attainable: Is it attainable by someone on the team?
- **R**—Relevant: Does it support the troop's vision and overall health?
- **T**—Time-based: When will the project be completed?

## SECTION 2: EFFECTIVE LEADERSHIP TODAY

### CHAPTER 6: Stages of Team Development

Every team goes through four distinct phases:

## Forming
Team members exhibit enthusiasm and anxiety. High dependence on the group leader for direction. Major issues: personal well-being, acceptance, and trust.

## Storming
Conflict develops as subgroups form with different goals. The leader's authority may be challenged. Major issues: power, control, and conflict.

## Norming
Members begin working together toward common goals. Increased collaboration and shared understanding. Group identity increases.

## Performing
Team members work effectively together with minimal supervision. The team challenges itself to succeed and often overachieves.

## Team Development Activities

### Forming-Stage Activities

**Beach Ball 411:** Write get-to-know-you questions on a beach ball. Toss the ball and have person answer question under their thumb.

**Commonalities:** Have pairs find three things they have in common, then combine pairs and find two things all four have in common.

### Storming-Stage Activities

**All Aboard:** Use rope to form circle on floor. Challenge group to stand inside circle with no body parts touching floor outside circle. Shrink circle and repeat.

**Human Knot:** Stand in circle, put hands in middle and grasp different people's hands, then unknot without releasing grip.

### Norming-Stage Activities

**Yurt Circle:** Stand in circle holding hands, lean out, then have "ones" lean in while "twos" lean out.

### Performing-Stage Activities

**Willow in the Wind:** One person in center with arms crossed falls in any direction while others redirect them gently around the circle.

### CHAPTER 7: Teaching EDGE and Leading EDGE

## The Teaching EDGE

**E** Explain: Tell them what to do
**D** Demonstrate: Show them what to do  
**G** Guide: Watch them do it
**E** Enable: Have them practice and/or teach it

The Teaching EDGE accounts for different learning styles:
- Auditory learners benefit from the Explain step
- Visual learners benefit from the Demonstrate step
- Kinesthetic learners benefit from the Guide step

## Zone of Proximal Development

The range of skills a learner can perform with assistance but not yet independently. There are tasks learners can do unaided, tasks they can do with guidance, and tasks beyond their current ability.

## Matching Teaching to Learning Stages

| Stage | Enthusiasm | Skill Level | Teaching EDGE |
|-------|------------|-------------|---------------|
| Forming | High | Low | Explain |
| Storming | Low | Low | Demonstrate |
| Norming | Rising | Growing | Guide |
| Performing | High | High | Enable |

## The Leading EDGE

Using EDGE with teams at different development stages:

**Forming (High Enthusiasm, Low Skills):** Explain what you expect them to do
**Storming (Low Enthusiasm, Low Skills):** Demonstrate how to succeed and show them the way
**Norming (Rising Enthusiasm, Growing Skills):** Guide by offering freedom to act while being ready to provide coaching
**Performing (High Enthusiasm, High Skills):** Enable team members to make decisions and progress on their own

### CHAPTER 8: Working With Youth Leaders

## How Adults Can Support Youth Leadership

- **It's the Scouts' troop:** Think of authority as a right you loan out, not a right you hold over youth leaders
- **The buck stops with the Scoutmaster:** The Scoutmaster makes the final call and shields youth leaders from criticism
- **Don't do for a youth leader what a youth leader should do**
- **Don't sandbag your youth leaders:** Support their decisions by providing necessary resources
- **Don't undermine your youth leaders' authority:** Redirect questions back to the appropriate youth leader
- **Never send an adult to do a Scout's job**
- **Model the behavior you expect**
- **Keep your distance:** Physical separation encourages Scouts to turn to youth leaders
- **Praise in public, criticize in private**

## Freedom to Fail

Youth leaders need the freedom to fail at tasks knowing that failure won't end the troop. This builds confidence and maturity.

### CHAPTER 9: Making the Patrol Method Work

## Making the Patrol Method Fail

Common mistakes that undermine the patrol method:

1. **Frequently Reorganizing Patrols:** Destroys patrol spirit, interrupts friendships, and ignores underlying problems
2. **Circumventing the Patrol Structure:** Abandoning patrol cooking or mixing patrols for convenience
3. **Going Around the Patrol Leader:** Adults taking over or Scouts bypassing the patrol leader undermines authority

## The Patrol Advisor

Assign a patrol advisor (assistant Scoutmaster) to each patrol to:
- Attend patrol meetings and outings
- Meet with patrol leader before and after meetings
- Help patrol leader prepare for patrol leaders' council meetings
- Monitor Scout advancement and attendance
- Encourage patrol activities and National Honor Patrol Award pursuit

## Honor Patrol Programs

Many troops create monthly honor patrol recognition based on point totals for achievements like:
- 10 points for each rank advancement
- 10 points for each new Eagle Palm
- 5 points for each merit badge
- 2 points for each member at troop meeting; 5 points if in full uniform
- 3 points for each official representative at patrol leaders' council meeting
- 10 points for planning or conducting a special patrol activity

## Patrol Meetings

### Patrol Meeting Agenda

1. **Opening:** Call to order or simple ceremony
2. **Roll call:** Scribe takes attendance
3. **Minutes:** Scribe reads log of last meeting
4. **Purpose:** Patrol leader announces current meeting purpose
5. **Recognition:** Advancement achieved by patrol members
6. **Business:** Plan activities, assignments, equipment check, vote on issues
7. **Games:** Play Scouting game
8. **Closing:** Brief closing thought by patrol leader

## Building Patrol Spirit

### Patrol Name
Patrol spirit starts with a name members select that fits their personalities or desired image.

### Patrol Emblem
Patrols typically choose stock patrol emblems but can create their own. The emblem can appear on equipment and patrol gear.

### Patrol Flag
Gives patrol something to rally around. Should be durable, easy to carry, and made by all patrol members.

### Patrol Yell
Can be simple word or complex call-and-response chant. Should be used regularly at meetings and events.

## SECTION 3: THE POWER OF PROGRAM AND HIGH ADVENTURE

### CHAPTER 10: Extraordinary Everyday Programs

## Troop Meetings

Most troop meetings should include eight parts:
- Preopening
- Opening
- Group instruction
- Skills instruction
- Patrol meetings
- Game
- Closing
- After the meeting

## Maintaining Balance: Age Groups

### New Scouts
Working toward First Class rank (within first 12-18 months). Everything is new experience. High energy but short attention span. Need clear guidance and opportunities to interact with other patrols.

### Experienced Scouts
Have been in troop 1-2 years, typically reached First Class. Understand troop operations. Need new challenges and variety in activities. May be serving in positions of responsibility.

### Older Scouts
Veterans with 4+ years in Scouts BSA. Need new challenges like high adventure, expanded leadership roles, or Eagle Scout rank. May see age gap with younger Scouts as insurmountable.

## Adding Life to Your Troop Meetings

Ways to energize meetings:
- **Meet outside:** Hold meetings at campfire circle or park
- **Use consultants:** Bring in Eagle Scouts, merit badge counselors, or expert leaders
- **Use technology:** Show videos, use smartphones for information
- **Tweak the schedule:** Adjust meeting plans based on needs
- **Provide resources:** Give youth leaders books and online resources for ideas

### CHAPTER 11: High Adventure

## Defining High Adventure

High-adventure experience spans multiple days and nights in isolated areas—backpacking, canoeing, mountain biking, climbing, skiing, rafting, kayaking, or other outdoor adventures. Typically limited to Scouts 15+ years old.

### Sample High-Adventure Activities

- Backpacking
- Bicycle touring
- Canoeing
- Climbing
- Horse packing
- Kayaking
- Mountain biking
- Rock climbing
- Scuba diving
- Snow camping
- Whitewater rafting
- Wilderness survival

## Why High Adventure?

### Challenge
Provides safer challenges for teenagers seeking to test physical and mental abilities.

### Fitness
Gives meaningful reward for becoming physically fit. Participants must be in good health verified by physical examination within one year.

### Leadership and Teamwork
Great opportunity for Scouts to practice leadership in patrol-sized crews with limited outside contact.

### Planning
Poor planning has serious consequences when far from trailhead. Teaches importance of thorough preparation.

### Program Focus
Preparation can fill months of troop meetings with meaningful activities related to the upcoming adventure.

## Planning a High-Adventure Trip

### Crew Capabilities Assessment

Ask these questions:
- Who will go on the trip?
- What are the ages and medical restrictions?
- How much camping and activity experience does crew have?
- Do crew members cooperate and work together?
- Does crew accept leadership?
- How well does crew deal with problems?
- Is crew committed to Leave No Trace principles?

### Crew Size
Best size is 8-10 members including adult advisors. Groups larger than 12 create logistics problems.

### Duration Planning
Include time for shakedowns, acclimatization, and unexpected delays. Allow generous time estimates for travel.

### Distance Guidelines

**Backpacking:** 2 miles per hour on flat trail, 1 mile per hour with heavy pack over rugged terrain
**Canoeing:** 7-12 miles per day including portages (Northern Tier guidelines)  
**Cycling:** 8-10 miles per hour including stops

## First-Aid and CPR Training

At least two people in every crew should have First Aid and CPR certification from recognized organizations such as:
- American Red Cross
- National Safety Council
- Wilderness Medical Associates
- National Outdoor Leadership School

## Emergency Communications

For every location, know:
- Location of nearest telephone or radio
- 24-hour availability of communications
- Consider satellite phones or personal locator beacons for remote areas

## SECTION 4: SERVICE AND STEWARDSHIP

### CHAPTER 12: Troop Service Projects

## Service and Advancement

Service hours required by rank:
- **Tenderfoot:** 1 hour
- **Second Class:** 2 hours
- **First Class:** 3 hours  
- **Star:** 6 hours
- **Life:** 6 hours (at least 3 conservation-related)
- **Eagle:** Service project demonstrating leadership

For Tenderfoot, Second Class, and First Class, Scouts must also explain how their service relates to the ideals of Scouting.

## Transformational Service Projects

Five attributes of effective projects:
- Have real significance
- Are Scout-planned
- Are clearly defined
- Are well-planned
- Are followed by reflection and recognition

To transform community, seek partner organizations with clear vision that truly need help. Scouts should work directly with people they serve alongside dedicated volunteers.

## A Sampling of Service Projects

- Conduct food drive for under-resourced school
- Build raised, wheelchair-accessible planter boxes for nursing home
- Create personal care kits for homeless people
- Collect toys and books for Ronald McDonald House
- Paint park benches and picnic tables
- Organize blood drive and recruit donors
- Help elementary students with reading
- Run bike-safety rodeo
- Build wheelchair ramps for homebound neighbors
- Plan holiday party for nursing home

## Making Service Projects Enjoyable

### Choose Reasonable Projects
First projects should be limited in scope and last no more than few hours. As Scouts gain experience, projects can become more ambitious.

### Consider Skill Levels
Projects should be within skill levels of participating Scouts while providing appropriate challenge for growth.

### Add Variety
Alternate between different tasks during day. Involve Scouts in all phases of project from planning to completion.

### Ensure Safety
Follow Scouting America's Service Project Safety Guidelines and Age Guidelines for Tool Use. Two registered adult leaders 21+ required at all activities.

## Recording Service Hours

For each project, record in advancement records:
- Date of project
- Number of registered Scouts participating
- Number of non-Scouts participating  
- Number of adult leaders participating
- Total hours worked
- Organizations that joined project
- Who benefited from project

### CHAPTER 13: Environmental Stewardship Projects

Stewardship is awareness of environment and willingness to care for it. Starts with following Leave No Trace and Tread Lightly! principles.

## Scouting America Distinguished Conservation Service Award

Recognizes outstanding conservation and environmental service efforts. Three award forms:
- **Youth:** Scouting America Distinguished Conservation Service Award
- **Adult:** Scouting America Distinguished Conservationist  
- **Organizations:** Certificate

## A Sampling of Environmental Stewardship Projects

- Plant trees and grasses for erosion control
- Remove invasive plant species
- Build bird feeders and nesting boxes
- Conduct stream-improvement projects
- Help maintain trails for various users
- Revegetate damaged areas
- Build erosion-control devices
- Clean up urban waterways
- Help stock lakes and streams
- Construct observation decks in wildlife refuges
- Install educational signs on nature trails
- Build fences to prevent overgrazing
- Establish urban greenbelts and parks

## Finding Places to Serve

Nearly one-third of United States is public domain. Contact:
- City and county parks
- State forests and parks
- National forests and monuments
- Wildlife refuges
- Council Scout camps
- Local conservation organizations

Many agencies have volunteer coordinators who can match groups with appropriate projects.

### CHAPTER 14: Eagle Scout Service Projects

## What the Eagle Scout Service Project Is—and Isn't

Requirements:
- Must benefit organization other than Scouting
- Cannot benefit individual (except in special circumstances)
- Must consist of more than routine labor
- Must demonstrate leadership of at least two other people
- No minimum number of hours required
- Does not require lasting value
- Does not require originality

## The Eagle Scout Project Workbook

Required for all Eagle candidates. Three major sections:
- **Project proposal:** Must be approved by unit leader, unit committee, beneficiary, and council/district
- **Project final plan:** Detailed planning (no approval required)
- **Project report:** Documents what was accomplished and lessons learned

## Project Approvals

Required approvals:
- **Project proposal:** Unit leader, unit committee, beneficiary, council/district
- **Fundraising application:** Beneficiary, unit leader, council/district  
- **Project report:** Beneficiary, unit leader

## SECTION 5: KEEPING SCOUTS INVOLVED AND INTERESTED

### CHAPTER 15: Advanced Advancement Advice

## The Purpose of Advancement

Advancement is one method of Scouting, not an end in itself. It's a vehicle to develop character, citizenship, and fitness.

## Teaching Life Lessons

Three life lessons advancement can teach:
1. **Hard work pays off:** Every Scout must meet same standard
2. **Working with adults:** Scouts work with merit badge counselors and other adults
3. **Becoming organized and self-motivated:** Advancement becomes individual responsibility

## Active Participation and Leadership

For Star, Life, and Eagle ranks, Scouts must be "active" for specified months and serve in position of responsibility.

### Defining Active Participation

Use three sequential tests:
1. **Scout is registered:** Youth is registered and considers themselves member of unit
2. **Scout is in good standing:** Not suspended for disciplinary reasons  
3. **Scout meets unit's reasonable expectations:** Meets preestablished activity expectations

Alternative if expectations not met: Scout may still be considered active if board of review can see sufficient involvement and positive contribution to Scouting's mission.

## Service Requirements by Rank

| Rank | Hours | Notes |
|------|-------|-------|
| Tenderfoot | 1 hour | Must explain relation to Scouting ideals |
| Second Class | 2 hours | Must explain relation to Scouting ideals |
| First Class | 3 hours | Must explain relation to Scouting ideals |
| Star | 6 hours | |
| Life | 6 hours | At least 3 conservation-related |
| Eagle | Leadership project | Plan, develop, give leadership |

## Merit Badges

Merit badge program introduces Scouts to 130+ different hobbies and vocations. Combines elements of textbook, apprenticeship, and expert instruction.

### Merit Badge Worksheets

Scouting America strongly discourages use of fill-in-the-blank worksheets. Merit badges should challenge thought processes and encourage interaction, not just completion of forms.

### Merit Badge Classes, Fairs, and Similar Events

When done well, these events can spur advancement and introduce new badges. Consider:
- Pick badges that work well in group settings
- Limit dedicated badge work during regular troop meetings
- Look for field-trip options
- Be satisfied with partial completion

## Scoutmaster Conferences

Required before each board of review. Not a test to pass but conversation between Scoutmaster and Scout about goals, participation, and understanding of Scouting ideals.

Must follow Youth Protection guidelines and be held in appropriate setting with proper supervision.

## Boards of Review

Purpose is to determine if Scout has met requirements and to encourage future participation. Should be encouraging experience that helps Scout reflect on accomplishments.

## Advancement Reporting

Report advancement via Scoutbook and Internet Advancement at least monthly, preferably more frequently. Digital reporting is more accurate and efficient than paper records.

# BSA Leadership and Program Operations Guide

## Boards of Review

After a Scout has completed the requirements for any rank starting with Tenderfoot, the Scout appears before a board of review. The board consists of at least three members of Scouting experience and decides whether they have fulfilled the requirements for the rank.

A Scout shall not be denied the opportunity for a board of review. When a Scout believes all the requirements for a rank have been met, the opportunity for a board of review must be granted. Scoutmasters—or councils or districts in the case of the Eagle Scout rank—may not prevent the Scout from having a board of review, or to "defer" the Scout, or to demand performance beyond the stated requirements in order to be granted one.

A board of review must consist of no fewer than three members and no more than six, all of whom must be at least 21 years of age. Scoutmasters and assistant Scoutmasters shall not serve on a board of review for a Scout in their own troop. However, they may serve on a board of review for a Scout in another troop or for their own children.

Boards of review for any rank are meant to be face-to-face, personal experiences. From time to time, however, as Scouts go off to college or the military, or live in very remote areas, or because of health or travel concerns, it may be impossible to hold in-person boards of review. In these rare situations where it is unreasonable to expect a Scout to travel long distances, it is permissible to use videoconferencing.

### What Happens at a Board of Review

A certain level of formality and meaningful questioning should exist, but it is important that the atmosphere be relaxed and that the review be conducted with the Scout Law in mind. Remember that a board of review is not an inquisition.

The unit leader may remain in the room, but only to observe, not to participate unless called upon. It is strongly recommended that the Scout's parents, relatives, or guardians should not be in attendance in any capacity—not as members of the board, as observers, or even as the unit leader's assistant.

A Scout must not be rejected at a board of review for reasons unrelated to advancement requirements. For example, a Scout must not be rejected just because the Scout did not bring their Scout's Handbook to the board of review, or because the Scout's uniform is not neat and clean.

### Board of Review Discussion Topics

Ask the Scout what he has learned, what skills they've gained, and what the Scout gained from fulfilling requirements. Discussion topics may include what leadership is and how the Scout's experience relates to leadership. Remember that though we have high expectations for our members, we do not expect perfection.

A board is not required to keep minutes, but it is a good idea. Any notes must remain confidential to the board. Once a review or appeal is completed, all notes must be destroyed.

## After the Board of Review

If the members agree the Scout is ready to advance, the Scout should be congratulated. The board of review date—not that of a subsequent court of honor—becomes the rank effective date.

If a board does not approve, the candidate must be told in writing what they can do to improve. The board should offer suggestions and direction for completing requirements properly.

# Working With Older Scouts

A youth who spends the maximum possible time as a Scout enters the program as a child and departs as an adult. Yet often, troop leaders treat Scouts who are in high school similarly to those who are in middle school. As a result, many older Scouts leave Scouting altogether.

## Developmental Characteristics of Older Scouts

### Middle Adolescence (ages 14 and 15)

Teenagers of 14 or 15:
- Are in the midst of high school
- May be struggling academically for the first time as courses become more challenging
- Are in the full throes of puberty
- May be as tall as many adults
- Are dating or at least interested in dating
- Are spending significant amounts of time away from home
- Are beginning to rely less on their parents and relying more on a peer group
- Yearn for the independence that they perceive will come with a driver's license and a job
- May be experimenting with behavior such as smoking or drinking alcohol
- Are continuing to experiment with their identity and may seem to be a different person in different settings
- Are developing a better sense of hypocrisy and adult failings
- Are better able than before to think abstractly, solve problems, and plan ahead

### Late Adolescence (ages 16 to 18)

Young adults of 16 to 18:
- Are almost fully grown physically and look more like adults than children
- Are in the later years of high school
- Are thinking (and worrying) about life after high school
- Realize the big decisions they're making now will affect the rest of their lives
- Want to prove to their parents and themselves that they're ready to be independent
- May have a job and access to a car, which gives them spending money, mobility, and independence
- Are spending more time than ever on homework, testing, school activities, and dating
- May have a steady romantic interest and a smaller circle of really good friends
- Can reason abstractly and think analytically nearly as well as adults
- Are becoming more interested in global politics, social issues, and religion
- Have a relatively stable identity, which is consistent across different settings

## Working With Scouts in Middle Adolescence

- Eliminate the words boy, girl, and kid from your vocabulary. Use Scout, young man, young woman, young adult, or older Scout instead.
- Set high—but achievable—expectations for their behavior.
- Remind Scouts of their status. If an older Scout does something that's inappropriate for their age, call them on it.
- Acknowledge Scouts' identity with high school. Accommodate scheduling conflicts.
- Create alternate peer groups in the troop. Leadership of an older-Scout patrol is a good way to help Scouts foster positive peer relationships.
- Encourage them to use their talents. Give older Scouts a chance to use their newfound abilities to think abstractly, problem-solve, and plan ahead.
- Understand that each Scout is different. Be tolerant of their experimentation.
- Encourage their participation in the wider world of Scouting.

## Working With Scouts in Late Adolescence

- Trust older Scouts as the near-adults they're becoming. Strive for a collegial relationship—not a leader-follower relationship.
- Acknowledge and accommodate your Scouts' high school identity. Plan around their schedules and activities.
- Acknowledge special efforts Scouts make to participate.
- Acknowledge their many achievements. Post the honor-roll list in your meeting place.
- Teach skills that are relevant to their stage of life.
- Harness teens' interest to tackle big topics and global issues.
- Show them connections between the merit badge program and what they're doing in school.
- Show them connections between Scouting and the real world.
- Figure out what their needs are and help to meet those needs.
- Take them places adults go. If they have been around for a while, they probably already have the skills you can teach them.
- Listen to them. They are seeking feedback, but in order to help them, you have to hear where they are coming from.

## Rites of Passage

Rites of passage are ceremonies or rituals that mark the transition from one stage of life to the next. Young people need rites of passage—so much so that they will invent them where they don't exist. The Ordeal and other ceremonies in the Order of the Arrow, Scoutmaster conferences, and the Court of Honor all serve as rites of passage.

**Physical hazing and initiations are prohibited and may not be included as part of any Scouting activity.**

# Working With the Order of the Arrow, Venturing, and Sea Scouts

## The Mission of the Order of the Arrow

The mission of the Order of the Arrow is to fulfill its purpose as Scouting's National Honor Society through cheerful service to others.

## Order of the Arrow Structure

The Order of the Arrow is organized into geographic levels: lodges, sections, and regions. At each level, the Order relies on youth leaders who are guided and supported by adult advisers.

**OA members age 18 through 20 must register as adults, complete Youth Protection training, undergo criminal background checks, and follow the same Youth Protection policies as older adults.**

## Order of the Arrow Membership Levels

**Ordeal Membership.** Once a Scout has been elected to the Order of the Arrow, the youth may complete their Ordeal at a resident camp, a camporee, or a special ceremony.

**Brotherhood Membership.** After at least 10 months of active membership as an Ordeal member, the Arrowman will be eligible to seal membership by completing additional requirements and participating in the Brotherhood ceremony.

**Vigil Honor.** The Vigil Honor is a high mark of distinction and recognition reserved for those Arrowmen who by exceptional service, personal effort, and unselfish interest, have made distinguished contributions beyond the immediate responsibilities of their position.

## How OA Members Are Identified

Arrowmen wear white sashes with a red arrow to signify their membership in the Order of the Arrow. The Ordeal sash shows an unbroken white sash with a red arrow pointing down. The Brotherhood sash includes a red bar above the arrow. The Vigil sash includes the Brotherhood sash with white triangle superimposed above the arrow.

It is appropriate for an Arrowman to wear the sash when doing official OA business or attending an OA ceremony. It is not appropriate to wear the sash at non-OA functions, such as troop meetings, camporees, courts of honor, Eagle courts of honor, or district or council programs.

## Unit Elections

To become a member of the Order of the Arrow, a Scout is first selected by the registered, active members in the Scout's unit. The Order of the Arrow lodge is responsible for conducting unit elections in every troop in the council every year.

At least 50 percent of the registered active unit membership must be present for an election to be valid. A Scout under age 21 at the time of election is eligible to vote in an Order of the Arrow unit election.

### Youth Membership Requirements

1. A Scout may hold the First Class rank or higher, be under the age of 21, and be registered as an active member of the troop.
2. The youth must have experienced 15 nights of camping during the two years preceding the election with the troop. These 15 nights must include one, but not more than one, long-term camp consisting of at least five consecutive nights of overnight camping. Only five nights of the long-term camping experience may be credited toward the 15-night camping requirement.

## Adult Candidate Nominations

Each year, upon holding a troop election for youth candidates, the unit committee may nominate registered adults, 21 years of age or older, to the lodge for induction. There can be no more than one-third of the number of youth candidates elected, rounded up. In addition to the one-third limit, the unit committee may also nominate the unit leader, as long as he or she has served as unit leader for at least the previous 12 months.

## OA Troop Representative

The Order of the Arrow troop representative is a youth serving a troop as the primary liaison to the lodge or chapter. This youth leader serves as a formal communication link between lodge activities and members in the troop.

### Order of the Arrow Troop Representative Position Description

- Encourage year-round and resident camping in the troop
- Encourage older Scout participation in high-adventure programs
- Encourage Scouts to actively participate in community service projects
- Encourage Arrowmen to assume positions of responsibility in the troop
- Encourage Arrowmen in the troop to be active participants in lodge activities
- Set a good example
- Wear the Scout uniform correctly
- Live by the Scout Oath, Scout Law, and OA Obligation
- Show Scout spirit

## OA Unit of Excellence Award

The Order of the Arrow Unit of Excellence Award identifies troops that demonstrate excellence in incorporating the OA into their annual planning.

### Award Criteria for Troops

1. **Leadership:** Implement the OA troop representative and OA troop representative adviser positions.
2. **Participation:** Have at least 50 percent of the troop's OA members attend at least one lodge event in addition to their Ordeal.
3. **Elections:** Schedule a unit election with the lodge or chapter election team annually.
4. **Planning:** Review the local OA calendar during annual program planning, and schedule troop events so that 50 percent or fewer of troop programs overlap with major lodge events.
5. **Conversion:** Have at least 25 percent of Ordeal members who are eligible seal their membership by converting to Brotherhood membership.

## Order of the Arrow Leadership Training

The OA offers training opportunities at multiple levels including Lodge Leadership Development, Section Training, National Leadership Seminar (NLS), and Philmont Training Center programs.

## OA High-Adventure Programs

The Order of the Arrow provides unique high-adventure opportunities for Arrowmen ages 14 and older:
- Order of the Arrow Crew at the Philmont Scout Ranch
- Order of the Arrow Wilderness and Canadian Odyssey
- Order of the Arrow Florida Sea Base
- Order of the Arrow Summit Experience

# Venturing and Sea Scouts

Unlike the Order of the Arrow, Venturing and Sea Scouts are distinct, standalone programs of Scouting America serving young adults—both male and female youth ages 14 through 20. (Youth who are 13 may join if they have completed the 8th grade.)

## Venturing's Aims

Venturing has five aims: character development, citizenship training, mental and physical fitness, leadership skills development, and fun and adventure.

## Venturing's Areas of Emphasis

All crews use adventure, leadership, personal growth, and service as areas of program emphasis. Venturing's program combines these four areas in the flexible Venturing program matrix.

## Venturing Recognition Program

The four Venturing levels constitute a pathway that guides a Venturer from participant to leader to mentor. The requirements are organized around Venturing's four areas of program emphasis.

**Venturing Award.** Designed to be earned within a month of a Venturer joining a crew. Requirements focus on getting oriented to the crew and making a commitment to participate.

**Discovery Award.** Designed to help a new Venturer transition from orientation to active participation.

**Pathfinder Award.** Designed to establish basic and intermediate leadership skills by providing opportunities to take on leadership in activities, adventures, and service projects.

**Summit Award.** The highest award in Venturing, designed to provide focused leadership skills and experience. Can be thought of as the Venturing equivalent to the Eagle Scout Award.

## Venturing Skills-Based Recognition: TRUST, Ranger, and Quest Awards

For Venturers seeking additional challenges in outdoor adventure, sports and physical fitness, or spiritual life, three additional awards are available:
- **Ranger Award:** Recognizes expertise in outdoor skills
- **Quest Award:** Provides objectives for Venturers to excel in their field of interest
- **TRUST Award:** For Venturers seeking spiritual requirements involving faith and service

**TRUST** is an acronym that stands for "Trusting your beliefs/faith, Respecting the beliefs of others, Understanding other cultures, Serving your community, and Taking action."

## Sea Scout Advancement

Sea Scouts has its own advancement track with four ranks:

**Apprentice.** Sea Scouts learn about ships organization, basic swimming and seamanship skills, safety and emergency procedures, and 16 hours of ship activities.

**Ordinary.** Requirements include advanced swimming, uniform knowledge, U.S. flag etiquette, Swimming merit badge, quarterdeck training, and seamanship skills.

**Able.** Requirements include advanced navigation, Lifesaving merit badge, seamanship progression, maritime environmental issues, and the Sea Scout Long Cruise (14-day cruise requirement).

**Quartermaster.** The highest award for Sea Scouts, presenting challenges similar to Eagle Scout. Requirements include intensification of skills learned at previous levels with significant additions in service projects, cruises, and weather/seamanship studies.

## Scout Advancement in Venturing and Sea Scouting

Scouts registered in Venturing or Sea Scouting may maintain dual registrations in a troop and continue working toward Eagle Scout until their 18th birthday.

# Discipline

A Scout is trustworthy, loyal, helpful, friendly, courteous, kind, obedient, cheerful, thrifty, brave, clean, and reverent—except when the Scout isn't. Because misbehavior is a given, your troop needs to be prepared to discipline Scouts.

## Defining Discipline

The word's literal meaning: to teach. Discipline teaches someone to follow a set of rules or to behave in a certain way. Consider these attributes of constructive discipline:
- Instead of focusing on past misdeeds, it focuses on future good behavior
- Instead of using punishment capriciously, it imposes natural consequences
- Instead of creating an adversarial relationship, it strengthens a caring relationship
- Instead of teaching pain avoidance, it teaches self-control

## The Guide to Safe Scouting and Discipline

**Discipline must be constructive:**
- Discipline must reflect Scouting's values
- Corporal punishment is never permitted
- Disciplinary actions involving isolation, humiliation, or ridicule are also prohibited

**Adult leaders are responsible for monitoring behavior and intervening when necessary.**

**Physical violence, sexual activity, emotional abuse, spiritual abuse, unauthorized weapons, hazing, discrimination, harassment, intimidation, bullying, initiation activities, pornography and inappropriate images, and theft have no place in the Scouting program and may result in revocation of membership.**

## Troop Codes of Conduct

The best approach lies somewhere in the middle. It's a good idea to make clear that Scouts can't bring alcohol, tobacco, illegal drugs, firearms, and fireworks on outings and to camps. Some troops have Scouts—and their parents—sign their code of conduct each year.

## Crafting Consequences

It's important to come up with consequences that are proportional to the misbehavior. In many cases, the best way to craft a response may well be to ask the offending Scout what consequences might be appropriate.

According to the Guide to Advancement, misbehavior is not a sufficient reason to deny a Scout a Scoutmaster conference or board of review.

Here are some consequences, roughly in order of severity:
- An apology to the offended person
- A conference with the Scoutmaster
- A written essay explaining how the actions violated the Scout Oath or Scout Law
- Restitution (such as paying for damage or replacement)
- A conference with the Scout's parents
- Removal from the current activity
- Not being allowed to participate in future activities
- Removal of special privileges
- Demotion from a position of responsibility
- Suspension or probation from the troop

## Involving Parents and the Local Council

The following must be reported to the Scout executive immediately:
- Any threat or use of a weapon
- Any negative behavior associated with race, religion, ethnicity, or disability
- Any reports of child abuse that meet reporting mandates
- Any violation of Scouting America's Youth Protection policy

# Counseling

As a Scout leader, you may conduct Scoutmaster conferences or sit on boards of review where Scouts discuss difficult issues. Because of situations like these, you should develop some basic counseling skills.

## Defining Counseling

Counseling is simply the process of listening to a person with a problem, helping with analysis of the situation and options. It requires an ability to listen and react in a way that helps others solve their own problems.

You should suggest professional counseling when:
- A Scout is at risk of inflicting self-harm
- A Scout is at risk of hurting someone else
- You suspect child abuse
- You suspect drug or alcohol abuse
- You see signs of depression, withdrawal, or hallucination
- A Scout's problem relates to sexuality or sexual or gender identity

## Elements of Good Counseling

Effective counseling can't happen in a crowded place or a noisy environment.
- Listen more than you speak
- Try to understand what the Scout is telling you
- Do not minimize or contradict what the Scout is saying
- Do not give quick, easy answers
- Support the Scout's thinking with further information and data

# Conflict Resolution

Conflict is inevitable in groups. Most conflict can be resolved cooperatively through mediation.

## Mediating Conflict

### Questions for Conflict Resolution

1. What do you want?
2. What are you doing to get it?
3. Is it working?
4. Do you want to figure out another way?

When you take a more directive approach, the questions become four statements:
1. This is what I want.
2. This is what I understand you are doing.
3. This is why that isn't working for me.
4. Here's what I need for you to do.

## Deciding Who Should Mediate Conflict

Generally speaking, it's someone who's close enough to the situation to understand what's going on but far enough removed to be impartial. Try to avoid having the Scoutmaster serve as the default mediator.

## Effective Communication in Conflict Situations

The contract between leaders and those who are led:

| My responsibility is: | Your responsibility is: |
|---|---|
| To do all I can to ensure your safety | To tell me when you don't feel safe |
| To help you get the most from your experience | To help me understand what you are learning |
| To be honest with you and treat you with respect | To be honest with me and treat me with respect |

# Special Troop Situations

## Small Troops

**Advantages:**
- Nimbleness in decision-making
- Ease of travel
- Speed in activities
- Flexibility in programming
- Focus on the individual
- Less equipment needed

**Challenges:**
- Programs for all ages
- Youth Leadership positions
- Limited resources

## Very Large Troops

**Characteristics:**
- Strong patrol method becomes essential
- Large cadres of adult leaders
- More complex Scoutmaster role
- Advanced advancement tracking needs
- Multiple leadership roles needed
- Focus on individual becomes challenging
- Better communication systems required
- Greater independence possible
- Easier integration of new members
- Need to build esprit de corps

## New Troops

**Key Focus Areas:**
- Develop a vision
- Develop a long-range plan
- Develop a strong first-year plan
- Plan key transition points
- Borrow expertise
- Consider a partner troop
- Insist on training
- Build up your bank account
- Connect with your chartered organization
- Emphasize uniforms
- Fly a troop flag
- Develop traditions
- Think about scalability
- Document everything

# Working With Underserved Populations

## Changes in Youth Demographics

The ethnic makeup of kids in the United States is shifting dramatically. As of 2020 projections, Hispanic youth represent 25.6% of the youth population, with continuing growth projected through 2060.

## Multicultural Marketing Techniques

1. Customize your message and resources
2. Use a community-based approach
3. Don't make assumptions about the target community
4. Remember: The messenger can be as important as the message
5. Understand that government agencies may not always be the best messengers
6. Build relationships with community leaders and organizations
7. Make a long-term commitment
8. Encourage volunteer participation from the community
9. Be culturally sensitive
10. Test materials with people from the target audience
11. Make people feel that you are talking to "people like me"
12. Enlist help from community organizations for translations
13. Use bilingual materials rather than separate single-language versions
14. Use community-based and neighborhood media outlets
15. Increase reach by using a variety of dissemination methods
16. Show goodwill by supporting small businesses in the target area

## Barriers to Participation

### Cost
- Keep fees and requests for money to a bare minimum
- Plan money-earning projects
- Avoid the image that Scouting costs a lot of money
- Be careful about deposits and registration fees
- Create camp scholarships
- Enlist support of the chartered organization

### Uniforms
- Have troop members start with only a Scout shirt or neckerchief
- Ask your council about used uniform programs
- Consider starting a troop uniform closet
- Have an organization adopt the troop to help with uniform costs

### Equipment
- Borrow from or partner with another troop
- Encourage Scouts to make do with school backpacks and hand-me-downs
- Enlist the chartered organization's help
- Ask local veterans' organizations to supply an American flag

## Understanding Diverse Populations

- All parents want the best for their children
- Every culture has its own set of norms and seeks programs that reinforce their values
- Cultures are not monolithic

## Scouting in Rural Communities

### Keys to Success in Rural Areas
- Bring 21st-century skills to rural youth
- Don't make assumptions about rural communities
- Present Scouting as their program, tailored to meet their needs
- Use the strength of organizations that have already gained community trust
- Adapt to local organizational styles

### Rural Challenges

**Driving Distances:** Consider monthly or quarterly meetings instead of weekly meetings, geographically organized patrols, and patrols organized by school-bus routes.

**Chartered Organization Support:** Consider multiple units under one organization, shared leadership among organizations, or group of citizens serving as chartered organization.

## Lone Scouts

The Lone Scout plan provides a Scouting opportunity for youth who cannot readily join a unit because of distance or other circumstances. Every Lone Scout must have a "friend and counselor" who agrees to guide the Scout.

## Awards for Working With Disadvantaged Scouts

- **William D. Boyce Service Award:** For distinguished contribution in providing Scouting for low-income rural or urban youth
- **Asian American Spirit of Scouting Service Award:** For involvement in Scouting opportunities for Asian American youth
- **Scoutingvale la Pena! Service Award:** For involvement in Scouting opportunities for Hispanic-American youth
- **Francis G. Guardipee Grey Wolf Award:** For distinguished service to American Indian youth in Scouting

# Working With Scouts With Special Needs and Disabilities

Since its founding in 1910, Scouting has had a long commitment to serve youth with physical, mental, and emotional disabilities. Experience has shown that Scouting works best when youth with disabilities are mainstreamed—placed in a regular troop.

## Council Advisory Committee

Most Scout councils have an advisory committee whose primary function is to better serve Scouts with physical and mental disabilities. This committee works with institutions that desire to charter Scout units and with existing units that have a Scout with a disability.

## Defining Disability

A disability is a physical or mental condition that impairs functioning in one or more areas: Physical, Learning, Cognitive, Emotional, Social.

According to the U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, one in 10 children in the United States has a disability.

### Common Disabilities in Scouting

**Autism spectrum disorder:** A neurological disorder whose signs usually appear early in childhood. Characteristics include difficulties with social interaction, verbal and nonverbal communication, and repetitive behaviors or interests.

**Cerebral palsy:** A group of disorders resulting from brain damage. Symptoms range from slight unsteadiness to total loss of control over movement or speech.

**Cognitive disabilities:** People with cognitive disabilities are limited in their ability to learn and function socially.

**Down syndrome:** A genetic disorder affecting physical and intellectual development.

**Emotional disturbance:** An inability to adjust to the problems of normal living.

**Learning disability:** A disorder in one or more basic psychological processes involved in understanding or using spoken or written language.

**Physical disability:** An impairment that hampers physical functioning.

**Visual impairment:** An inability to see normally even with corrective lenses.

**Hearing impairments:** Including both prelingual deafness (before learning speech) and postlingual deafness (after developing speech).

## The Benefits of Serving Scouts With Disabilities

- Youth with special needs often get more out of the program than other kids do
- Scouting can be the bridge between school and community
- Troops benefit when Scouts with disabilities are fully integrated
- All members learn that disability is not a black-and-white issue

## Working With Scouts With Disabilities

The best guide is to use good common sense. Begin with the Scout and the Scout's parent(s); seek guidance from them on how best to work with the Scout.

**Basic premises:**
- Every Scout wants to participate fully and be treated and respected like every other member
- To the fullest extent possible, Scouts with disabilities should be given opportunities to camp, hike, and take part in other activities
- Many Scouts with disabilities can accomplish basic Scouting skills but may require extra time

## Parents' Prejoining Conference

Prior to joining a troop, parents and the Scout should meet with the troop leader. Issues to discuss:

**General Characteristics:** Scout's strengths and weaknesses
**Unit Operation:** How the Scouting program works
**Physical Disabilities:** Limitations and accommodations needed
**Mental Disabilities:** Present capabilities and skill levels
**Medication:** What the Scout takes regularly
**Behavior and Discipline:** Any behavioral issues and appropriate responses
**Diet and Eating Problems:** Special diets or restrictions
**Living Skills:** Ability to attend to personal needs
**Transportation:** Parents' responsibility
**Emergency Procedures:** Medical contacts and history

## Guidelines for Specific Types of Disabilities

### Mobility Impairments
- Never move equipment out of the person's reach
- Always ask before helping
- Position yourself at eye level for conversations

### Hearing Loss
- Make sure the person is looking at you before speaking
- Speak slowly and enunciate clearly
- Use gestures and visual demonstrations
- Only one person should speak at a time

### Vision Impairments
- Identify yourself by name
- Offer your arm, but don't try to lead the person
- Describe surroundings when helping
- Don't distract guide dogs

### Speech/Language Disorders
- Stay calm and be patient
- Don't shout
- Give your full attention
- Model slow speech with short phrases

### Cognitive Disabilities
- Be clear and concise
- Don't use complex sentences or difficult words
- Don't talk down to the person
- Be understanding of frustration

### Autism Spectrum Disorder
- Provide structured, consistent, predictable routines
- Be patient and allow extra time
- Provide visual schedules
- Give information about new activities ahead of time
- Break tasks into smaller steps

### Attention Deficit Disorder
- Structure Scout meeting time, activities, and rules
- Be positive and praise appropriate behavior
- Make learning interesting
- Use charts to monitor behavior
- Be clear in advancement planning

### Learning Disabilities
- Use praise and encouragement to build self-esteem
- Use short, direct instruction
- Stay on regular schedules
- Give extra time when needed

## Advancement for Scouts With Disabilities

Most Scouts with disabilities are able to advance with proper guidance and support. Several forms of accommodation are available.

### Registration Beyond the Age of Eligibility

The council executive board may grant a permanent time extension beyond age 18 for Scouts with permanent disabilities.

**Required documentation:**
1. Letter from parent/guardian describing the disability
2. Completed youth membership application
3. Annual Health and Medical Record (parts A, B, and C)
4. Signed statement from qualified health professional
5. Letter from unit leader supporting the request
6. Other supporting documentation as appropriate

### Alternative Requirements for Tenderfoot, Second Class, and First Class Ranks

A Scout with permanent physical or mental disability that prevents completion of standard requirements may request alternative requirements.

**Procedures:**
1. Do as many standard requirements as possible
2. Gather supporting letters and statements
3. Prepare written request for alternative requirements
4. Advancement committee reviews the request

### Alternative Merit Badges for Eagle Scout Rank

Though individual requirements for merit badges may not be modified, Scouts with special needs may request approval for alternative badges they can complete.

**Procedures:**
1. Letter from unit leader describing the disability
2. Candidate must earn as many required merit badges as ability permits
3. Complete Application for Alternative Eagle Scout Rank Merit Badges
4. Alternative merit badge must demand as much skill and effort as those replaced
5. Physical activities must be approved by Scout's health-care provider
6. Application must be approved by council advancement committee

## Challenges You May Face

- Parental overprotection may need sensitive handling
- Other unit members may be uncomfortable initially
- Some activities may be beyond capabilities or unit's ability to safely support
- Medication considerations during activities
- Extra effort required but benefits are worth it for everyone involved

# Program Features (all 3 volumes)

# SCOUTS BSA PROGRAM FEATURES VOLUME 1

## PROGRAM FEATURES FOR TROOPS AND CREWS

### Volume 1 Contents
- Camping
- Climbing and Rappelling  
- Communication
- Cycling
- First Aid
- Games
- Geocaching
- Hiking
- Outdoor Ethics
- Paddle Sports
- Pioneering
- Rifle Shooting
- Safety
- Science
- Snowboarding and Skiing
- Spectator Sports

## Annual Program Planning

Planning is a two-phase process:
- **Long-term planning** results in an annual calendar and unit goals
- **Short-term planning** yields detailed plans for one month's meetings and outings

### The Annual Planning Conference

Held in late spring or early summer after school, community, and council calendars are published.

**Step 1—Gather necessary information:**
- Chartered organization's key dates
- Personal dates that may affect activities
- Key district and council dates
- Data from troop resource survey or activity interest survey
- Last year's annual plan
- Unit priorities and goals
- Advancement records for each member
- General outline of next year's program

**Step 2—** Discuss the planning process with your top youth leader and prepare a draft outline.

**Step 3—** Share the draft plan with other youth leaders for input using the "start, stop, continue" method:
- What should we start doing that we are not currently doing?
- What should we stop doing that is not working?
- What should we continue doing that is working well?

**Step 4—** Invite the following to attend the conference:
- Unit committee members and other adult leaders
- Chartered organization representative
- Unit commissioner (optional)
- Anyone else who might be helpful

**Active roles:** unit leader, assistant unit leaders, and all members in Venturing.

### Holding the Annual Planning Conference

Can be held in 3-4 hours on a Saturday morning or turned into an overnight retreat.

**Materials needed:**
- Large calendar showing the full year
- Program planning chart worksheet
- Draft plan and important dates

### The Annual Planning Conference, Step by Step

**Step 1—** Unit leader leads discussion on goals for the coming year.

**Step 2—** Share the draft calendar showing researched dates and events.

**Step 3—** Discuss and vote to approve the dates and activities.

**Step 4—** Share updates from members about desired programs, using program features as foundation.

**Step 5—** Add other important dates:
- Unit meetings and leadership meetings
- Boards of review
- Courts of honor
- Elections
- Open house
- Service projects
- Joint outings and transition ceremonies

**Step 6—** Hold final discussion and vote for approval.

**Step 7—** Share the plan with unit families, chartered organization, and other interested parties.

### After the Planning Conference

Prepare the calendar with these features:
- Include as much detail as possible
- Indicate when regular meetings won't be held
- Color-code events for easy reference
- Add revision date at bottom of every page

### Monthly Program Planning

Each month, the patrol leaders' council (or crew officers) plan in detail:
- Review the month that's just ending
- Plan everything for the month beginning
- Preview the following month and make assignments

## How to Use the Program Features

Each program feature provides:
- General information about the topic
- Related advancement requirements and awards
- Games related to the topic
- Ideas for using the EDGE method
- Four weeks' worth of meeting plans
- Outlines of three main events (outings)
- Print and online resources

**Icons:**
- **Essential:** Basic skills-development and beginner activities
- **Challenging:** Skills-development for those beyond basics
- **Advanced:** Learning topics for those ready for ultimate challenge

### A NOTE TO VENTURERS

For Discovery and Summit awards, main event examples are considered:
- **Essential (Tier I):** Beginner level activities, often less than full day
- **Challenging (Tier II):** Intermediate activities requiring planning, lasting more than four days
- **Advanced (Tier III):** High-challenge activities requiring extensive preparation, lasting at least four days

# CAMPING

## Living Comfortably in the Outdoors

Camping is the foundation of many other Scouting activities. This month's meetings and main event will help Scouts develop good camping skills.

### Objectives

This month's activities should:
- Instill knowledge and skills to be comfortable in camp
- Emphasize outdoor ethics to protect the environment
- Teach knots and when to use them
- Help Scouts understand outdoor shelters
- Teach importance of keeping camp clean
- Foster sense of communion with nature and God
- Build self-confidence by learning and demonstrating skills
- Help Scouts work cooperatively in small groups while living outdoors

### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

- Tenderfoot requirements 1a, 1b, 1c, 2a, 2b, 2c, 3b, and 3c
- Second Class requirements 1a, 1b, 1c, 2a, and 2d
- First Class requirements 1a and 3d
- Camping merit badge
- Firem'n Chit

## Outdoor Ethics

Follow principles of both Leave No Trace and Tread Lightly!

**Leave No Trace principles:**
- Plan ahead and prepare
- Travel and camp on durable surfaces
- Dispose of waste properly
- Leave what you find
- Minimize campfire impacts
- Respect wildlife
- Be considerate of other visitors

**Tread Lightly! principles:**
- Travel responsibly
- Respect the rights of others
- Educate yourself
- Avoid sensitive areas
- Do your part

## Three-Bin Dishwashing

Use three wash bins: hot soapy water, clean cold rinse water, and hot water with bleach as sanitizer. Scrape excess food, wash in first bin, rinse in second, immerse in third, then air dry.

## The Trucker's Hitch

Provides mechanical advantage and works like a pulley system. Useful for securing loads and tightening ridge lines.

### THE KODIAK CHALLENGE

The advanced main event is a special opportunity for adults and older Scouts (age 16+) to develop leadership skills during a 3-6 day trek. Designed to push boundaries and encourage participants to try new things outside their comfort zones while applying BSA leadership skills.

## Main Events Summary

| ESSENTIAL | CHALLENGING | ADVANCED |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| Overnight car camp—Find a campsite you can drive to and camp overnight to hone camping skills | Camping in the teens—Return to Scouting's early days using techniques from earliest Scout manuals | Kodiak Challenge—Complete the challenge, a 5-6 day trek adventure reinforcing leadership skills |
