# CLIMBING AND RAPPELLING

## Go Vertical!

Climbing and rappelling are among the most exhilarating activities in Scouting, combining physical challenges with mental problem-solving.

### Objectives

This month's activities should:
- Teach principles of Climb On Safely
- Show how to identify climbing safety hazards
- Help Scouts become familiar with climbing equipment
- Teach knots used in climbing
- Demonstrate difference between climbing and rappelling
- Let Scouts learn and demonstrate climbing and rappelling skills

### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

- Tenderfoot requirement 1b
- Second Class requirement 1a
- First Class requirement 1a
- Camping merit badge requirement 8(b)
- Climbing merit badge
- Ranger: Mountaineering elective

## Climb On Safely

BSA's procedures for organizing climbing and rappelling activities. Includes eight core points:

**1. Qualified Supervision**—Mature adult at least 21 years old who understands risks and is trained in Climb On Safely compliance.

**2. Qualified Instructors**—At least 21 years old and trained in specific type of climbing. Minimum of two instructors for all activities (up to 12 participants).

**3. Physical Fitness**—Evidence of fitness and current BSA Annual Health and Medical Record required.

**4. Safe Area**—All activities must use established climbing site. Each participant must wear UIAA- or CE-approved rock-climbing helmet. Everyone must be anchored when within 8 feet of falling hazard.

**5. Equipment**—Proper equipment verified by climbing instructor. Helmets, harnesses, rope, and hardware must meet appropriate requirements.

**6. Planning**—Share plan with parents and unit committee, including permission for land use and qualified instructor assistance.

**7. Environmental Conditions**—Monitor for loose rock, poisonous plants, wildlife, and inclement weather.

**8. Discipline**—All participants must know, understand, and respect rules and procedures.

### Climb On Safely Training

Available to Scouts and Venturers at least 13 years old. Issues Climb On Safely card valid for two years.

### Activity Limitations

**Scouts:**
- Bouldering no higher than shoulder height with spotters
- Top-rope climbing with trained belayers
- Rappelling with trained belayers
- Belaying with supervision and backup

**Older Scouts and Venturers:**
- Above activities plus belaying with supervision
- Practicing lead climbing with top-rope belay

## Main Events Summary

| ESSENTIAL | CHALLENGING | ADVANCED |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| Trip to indoor climbing gym—Get beginners comfortable on climbing walls | Outdoor climbing—Go to natural climbing area with varied routes | Climbing competition—Compete in organized event with lead, speed, and bouldering |

# COMMUNICATION

## Getting and Giving Information

Communication is an essential life skill. This month's activities will help Scouts use various communication techniques more effectively.

### Objectives

This month's activities should:
- Help Scouts become better communicators and listeners
- Introduce variety of communication methods
- Help understand and overcome communication barriers
- Emphasize importance of internet safety
- Encourage pursuit of communication-related awards
- Introduce careers in communication field
- Build self-confidence through learning and demonstrating skills

### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

- Tenderfoot requirement 1b
- Second Class requirement 1a
- First Class requirements 1a, 9a, and 9b
- Communication, Digital Technology, Journalism, Public Speaking, Radio, and Theater merit badges
- Interpreter strip
- Personal Safety Awareness
- Ranger: Communications core requirement

## What Is Communication?

Communication is sharing information with others using symbols—words or images that stand for something else.

**Language** is the most common way we use symbols to communicate verbally and in writing.

**Nonverbal communication** includes body language, gestures, and facial expressions.

### Interpreter Strip

Have you studied a foreign language? You could earn an interpreter strip by:
- Carrying on five-minute conversation
- Translating two-minute speech
- Writing a letter in the language
- Translating 200 words from written word

## Main Events Summary

| ESSENTIAL | CHALLENGING | ADVANCED |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| Communication field trip—Visit newspaper office, ad agency, or communications workplace | Communication hike—Take hike using communication barriers to illustrate importance of effective communication | Merit badge event—Camp and work on Communication merit badge |

# CYCLING

## Getting Stronger, Going Further

Bicycling combines exercise, travel, and recreation. Few activities are better than cycling for aerobic exercise with minimal joint impact.

### Objectives

This month's activities should:
- Show Scouts how to ride safely
- Teach cycling first aid
- Teach bicycle maintenance and repair
- Show how to plan rides and routes
- Teach good cycling nutrition
- Introduce cycling organizations
- Teach about cycling gear and clothing

### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

- Tenderfoot requirement 1b
- Second Class requirements 1a and 3a
- First Class requirements 1a and 10
- Cycling and Personal Fitness merit badges
- 50-Miler Award
- Ranger: Cycling/Mountain Biking elective
- Quest: Requirement 5

## Bicycling Safety

**Helmet fitting:** Should sit level on head with front edge no more than one inch above eyebrows. Side straps should form V under ears when buckled.

**Bike fit:** When straddling top tube, there should be 1-2 inches of space for road bikes, 2-4 inches for mountain bikes.

### Nutrition

Good nutrition is critical for long-distance riding:
- Stay hydrated with water
- Energy drinks may be appropriate for rides over 10 miles
- Carbohydrates supply necessary fuel
- Force yourself to eat and drink—thirst and hunger may not be reliable guides

### Training Plan: Four Weekend Rides for Cycling Merit Badge

**Weekend 1:** 20 miles, 300 feet elevation gain, 10-12 mph average
**Weekend 2:** 30 miles, 500 feet elevation gain, 12-13 mph average  
**Weekend 3:** 40 miles, 800 feet elevation gain, 13-14 mph average
**Weekend 4:** 50 miles, 1,000 feet elevation gain, 14-15 mph average

## Main Events Summary

| ESSENTIAL | CHALLENGING | ADVANCED |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| Out-and-back bike ride—Round trip ride lasting 4-5 hours with rest stops | 100-mile bike trip—Century ride from campsite or 50 miles to camp, stay overnight, 50 miles back | Cycling trek—Multi-day ride carrying all gear, covering 75 miles per day |

---

# FIRST AID

## Prepared to Help When Needed

First aid is caring for injured or ill persons until they receive professional medical care. It's an important skill for every Scout.

### Objectives

This month's activities should:
- Teach Scouts how to evaluate a scene
- Help understand first-aid needs on outings
- Teach what to say when calling 911
- Cover basic first-aid skills needed for advancement
- Encourage Scouts to work together as team
- Prompt pursuit of additional first-aid training

### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

- Tenderfoot requirements 1a, 4a, 4b, 4c, and 4d
- Second Class requirements 1a, 6a, 6b, 6c, 6d, and 6e
- First Class requirements 1a, 7a, 7b, 7c, 7d, and 10
- Merit badges: Emergency Preparedness, First Aid, Safety, Search and Rescue, Wilderness Survival
- Ranger: First Aid core requirement

## The First Responder

**Initial assessment includes:**
- Safety (yours and victim's)
- Mechanism of injury
- Medical information devices
- Number of casualties
- Available bystanders

**Universal precautions:**
- Always avoid contact with blood or body fluids
- Use gloves whenever possible
- Use face shield for mouth-to-mouth resuscitation

### Remember Your ABCDs

**A is for airway:** Place person on back and open airway by tilting head back
**B is for breathing:** Look, listen, feel for breathing up to 10 seconds
**C is for circulation:** Begin CPR if person doesn't breathe after rescue breaths
**D is for defibrillation:** Use AED if trained and available

### Calling 911

Write down before calling:
- Location of victim
- Description of injury or illness
- Time injury occurred
- Treatment victim has received
- Number of people with victim and their skill level
- Requests for special assistance

Don't hang up until operator tells you it's OK.

### Triage

Quickly checking each victim for injuries and determining how to use available first-aid resources.

## Main Events Summary

| ESSENTIAL | CHALLENGING | ADVANCED |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| First-Aid Clinic—Half-day workshop to brush up on first-aid skills | First-Aid Championship—During campout, set up realistic first-aid scenarios | Community Disaster Drill—Participate in actual community casualty drill |

## ARCHERY

### On Target

Movies like *The Hunger Games*, *The Avengers*, and *Brave* have brought renewed interest to archery. As a result, archery has become one of the fastest growing sports in the United States. This program feature will describe the equipment and techniques used in archery and will help you understand what it takes to become a proficient archer.

#### Objectives
This month's activities should:
- Teach Scouts how to handle bows and arrows safely
- Explain the different types of bows and how to take care of each type
- Teach Scouts about the parts of different bows and arrows
- Instruct Scouts in the fundamentals of archery as they apply to the bow that is being used
- Introduce Scouts to the various styles of archery that are available to create a lifelong hobby

#### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS
- Archery merit badge
- Ranger: Shooting Sports elective
- Venturing Shooting Sports Outstanding Achievement Award
- USA Archery Junior Olympic Archery Development Awards

#### Leadership Planning
As a leadership team, you may want to discuss the following items at planning meetings when choosing archery as your program feature:

1. What is our unit's current skill level?
2. Who do we know who is an archery instructor?
3. What materials and equipment (bows, arrows, quivers, etc.) do we need?
4. Do we have a copy of Scouting America's current National Shooting Sports Manual?
5. What location is needed for meetings—our normal meeting place or a shooting range?
6. Is there any cost factor involved?
7. What will we do for our main event, and where will we go?
8. To meet our needs, what should we change in the sample meeting plans?

#### SUPERVISION
All archery instruction and archery range management must be conducted by a trained, qualified range master who is certified in archery range safety and instruction. To qualify as an archery range master, the range master must be at least 18 years old.

#### GENERAL ARCHERY SAFETY RULES

• Keep all arrows in their quivers until ready to shoot.
• The safest way to transport an arrow is in its quiver. If you must carry arrows in your hands, hold them securely with both hands around all of the arrows.
• Be sure the area around and beyond your target is clear before you shoot. Never draw a bow if anyone is in front of the shooting line.
• Always aim and shoot at a definite target; never shoot an arrow into the air.
• Always have an arrow on the string when drawing a bow. Dry firing (shooting a bow without an arrow) can seriously damage a bow and possibly injure the archer.
• Always use proper safety equipment, including an arm guard and a finger tab or glove.
• Always inspect your equipment before shooting. Repair or replace damaged equipment.
• Never shoot an arrow straight up in the air.
• Walk, do not run, on the archery range.
• When retrieving arrows from behind a target, lean your bow against the face of the target or stick an arrow in the top of the target with the nock attached to it to let other archers know that you are behind the target.

#### TYPES OF BOWS

A compound bow has a system of strings and cables connected to cams (pulleys) of various designs. When the bowstring is drawn back, the cams multiply the force the archer exerts, storing more energy. Most modern compound bows have a let-off at full draw and use sights to aid in aiming.

Most traditional bows have no sights and are shot instinctively. There are several types of traditional bows, with the recurve and the longbow being the most common.

A crossbow is shot like a rifle. There is a stock that holds the bow horizontally. The crossbow shoots projectiles called "bolts"—that are much shorter and heavier than regular arrows.

#### COMPETITION TYPES

**3-D archery** competition uses molded-foam replicas of different game animals. The season for 3-D archery is year-round.

**The indoor archery season** lasts from late October through March. You aim at a multi-colored target face at 20 yards.

**Field archery** competition includes three rounds, all on a roving course. The season generally runs from March through July.

**Target archery** is the most familiar competition, and the type seen in the Olympics. Target archery involves shooting at multi-colored targets in an open field.

---

## BACKPACKING

### The World at Your Feet

When you go backpacking, you carry all the essentials of home on your back. You might hike for miles along a remote mountain ridge, spend a night in a secluded valley, have lunch along a winding trail, and enjoy dinner by starlight on a mountaintop.

#### Objectives
This month's activities should:
- Improve physical fitness
- Develop practical skills in living with nature and God
- Foster a greater appreciation for the outdoors and a determination to follow the Outdoor Code and the principles of Leave No Trace
- Offer opportunities to practice planning and teamwork
- Strengthen self-confidence and team building

#### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS
- Scout requirement 1e
- Tenderfoot requirements 1a, 1b, 5a, 5b, and 5c
- Second Class requirements 1a, 1b, 3a, and 3b
- First Class requirements 1a, 1b, and 1d
- Backpacking and Hiking merit badges
- 50-Miler Award
- Outdoor Ethics awards

#### The Principles of Leave No Trace

1. **Plan Ahead and Prepare.** Comply with area regulations such as observing limitations on group size. Camp only in designated areas or areas of high use. Obtain all necessary permits.

2. **Travel and Camp on Durable Surfaces.** In high-use areas, concentrate your activities where vegetation is already absent. In less-traveled areas, spread out to avoid creating trails that cause erosion.

3. **Dispose of Waste Properly.** Carry out all trash, leftover food, and litter. Use biodegradable soap and properly dispose of dishwater at least 200 feet from water sources.

4. **Leave What You Find.** Preserve the sense of discovery. Leave rocks, plants, and other objects as you find them.

5. **Minimize Campfire Impacts.** Use lightweight camp stoves for cooking. After dinner, enjoy a candle lantern instead of a fire.

6. **Respect Wildlife.** Observe wildlife from afar. Store food securely and never feed wildlife.

7. **Be Considerate of Other Visitors.** Protect the quality of their experience by respecting their privacy. Select campsites away from other groups. Keep your noise level down.

## CITIZENSHIP

### Doing Your Civic Duty

What does it mean to be a citizen? People often talk about the rights they have as citizens, but citizenship also comes with responsibilities, such as voting and paying taxes. Every opportunity you have to provide service to your country strengthens our nation.

#### Objectives
This month's activities should:
- Teach Scouts to give back to their community
- Give Scouts a better understanding of how the government works
- Introduce Scouts to local, state, and national government leaders
- Demonstrate how the election process works
- Let Scouts experience how Congress passes laws
- Let Scouts visit a national or state park, historical monument, state capitol, or the U.S. Capitol

#### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS
- Tenderfoot requirements 7a and 7b
- Second Class requirements 8a, 8b, and 8e
- First Class requirements 8a, 8c, and 8d
- Citizenship in the Community, Citizenship in the Nation, and Citizenship in the World merit badges
- Discovery: Service requirement 6

#### Branches of Government
The United States government has three branches. The legislative branch makes the laws. The executive branch enforces and administers the laws. The judicial branch interprets and applies the laws.

#### Community Service
Community Service means service that benefits the people or area around you, and it is a key part of the Scouting program. Giving back and taking care of your community is part of being a good citizen.

#### POLICY ON SCOUT PARTICIPATION IN POLITICAL EVENTS
Uniformed unit members and leaders may participate in flag ceremonies at political events and may lead the Pledge of Allegiance; however, they should retire after the ceremony and not remain on the speakers' platform. Photos of candidates with Scouts in uniform are not allowed in political campaign materials of any kind.

## ENGINEERING

### Building Our World

Engineers work to solve the difficult problems that face our society. Whether it's building a bridge to cross a river, creating a new machine to manufacture cars, or fitting technology into a smartphone, an engineer's work can leave a lasting impact on society.

#### Objectives
This month's activities should:
- Define the different branches of engineering
- Help Scouts understand what engineers do
- Demonstrate key engineering concepts
- Help Scouts explore the different career paths available to them
- Demonstrate real-world applications of engineering principles

#### DIFFERENT TYPES OF ENGINEERS

Engineers work to find solutions to problems. Engineering can be broken down into four major branches:

**Chemical engineering**—the study of chemicals and the process of creating new materials and compounds

**Mechanical engineering**—the study of designing mechanical systems

**Civil engineering**—the study of designing and constructing public and private works

**Electrical engineering**—the study of designing electrical systems and components

## FISHING

### Catch Me If You Can

Fishing is a delightful way to get outdoors, to match wits with nature, to spend time with your friends, and even to keep yourself fed. Scouting's founder, Robert Baden-Powell, was an avid fly fisherman and a pioneer of "catch and release" fishing.

#### Objectives
This month's activities should:
- Introduce basic fishing skills to Scouts who have limited exposure to fishing
- Improve the fishing skills of Scouts at all levels of experience
- Give each Scout a reasonable chance for success by teaching them how to find fish
- Teach Scouts the important fishing skills, including how to rig their outfits effectively
- Review important knots, including where and why each is used
- Teach Scouts how to release their catch or prepare it for cooking
- Cover possible risks and health threats while fishing
- Emphasize conservation and the role that Leave No Trace guidelines play in sport fishing

#### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS
- Tenderfoot requirement 1b
- Second Class requirements 1a and 4
- First Class requirements 1a, 4b, and 10
- Fish and Wildlife Management, Fishing, and Fly-Fishing merit badges
- Complete Angler recognition

#### Staying Safe While Fishing
1. Always use the buddy system
2. Understand when you will meet or return
3. Dress appropriately for the weather
4. Be observant to ensure that no one is in harm's way while casting
5. Watch for steep inclines at the water's edge and for rocks and stumps while wading
6. If someone is hooked, all parties must stop and not move
7. Safety glasses or sunglasses are highly recommended
8. Drink plenty of water while fishing to avoid dehydration
9. A fishing rod can become a lightning rod. During thunderstorms, set your rod down until the weather clears

#### Fishing Knots
A chain is only as strong as its weakest link, and a fishing line is only as strong as its knots. Several knots serve specific purposes:

- The **arbor knot** attaches fishing line to the reel
- The **double surgeon's loop** creates a secure open loop at the end of a line
- The **blood knot** attaches two lengths of monofilament lines together
- The **improved clinch knot** attaches the terminal line to a hook, lure, or swivel
- The **Palomar knot** attaches a hook or lure to the terminal line

## FITNESS AND NUTRITION

### Getting Into Shape

Fitness is essential to many Scouting activities and a major contributor to a long, healthy life. The Scout Oath proclaims that a Scout is physically strong. This program feature will help Scouts learn about fitness and nutrition.

#### Objectives
This month's activities should:
- Define the importance of fitness
- Discuss the components of good nutrition
- Explain how wellness is related to fitness
- Emphasize the dangers of drugs, alcohol, and tobacco
- Teach positive ways to reduce stress
- Show Scouts how to work toward lifetime fitness

#### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS
- Tenderfoot requirements 6a, 6b, and 6c
- Second Class requirements 2b, 7a, 7b, and 7c
- First Class requirement 2a
- Athletics, Cooking, Personal Fitness, Public Health, and Sports merit badges

#### GET FIT
You can improve your fitness by changing the frequency, intensity, time, and type of exercises you do:

**Frequency:** How many times do you exercise each week or month?

**Intensity:** How strenuous is the exercise in terms of weight lifted, speed of movement, or effort expended?

**Time:** How long do you do an exercise per session?

**Type:** What kinds of exercise do you do?

#### THE MYPLATE WAY
The U.S. government created MyPlate to help Americans learn better eating habits. Here are 10 tips from the U.S. Department of Agriculture:

1. **Find your healthy eating style.** Creating a healthy style means regularly eating a variety of foods to get the nutrients and calories you need.
2. **Make half your plate fruits and vegetables.**
3. **Focus on whole fruits.**
4. **Vary your veggies.**
5. **Make half your grains whole grains.**
6. **Move to low-fat or fat-free milk or yogurt.**
7. **Vary your protein routine.**
8. **Drink and eat beverages and food with less sodium, saturated fat, and added sugars.**
9. **Drink water instead of sugary drinks.**

# CAVING

## Caves and Safety

### Caving Equipment and Techniques

**Essential equipment for caving:**
- Three sources of light per person (helmet light and two handheld flashlights)
- Helmet
- Gloves
- Sturdy boots or shoes with good traction
- Clothing that can get dirty and torn
- Small pack
- Water
- Energy snacks

**Cave formations to protect:**
- Stalactites (hang from ceiling)
- Stalagmites (grow from floor)
- Flowstone
- Cave pearls
- Draperies

### White Nose Syndrome Prevention

White-nose syndrome is a disease that has killed millions of bats. To prevent spreading it:
- Follow all decontamination protocols
- Do not enter caves where bats are present during hibernation season
- Clean all clothing and equipment between cave visits
- Follow guidelines from cave managers and the NSS

### Cave Ethics

**Take nothing but pictures, leave nothing but footprints, kill nothing but time:**
- Never break or touch cave formations
- Pack out all trash, including organic waste
- Do not disturb wildlife
- Stay on established paths when possible
- Keep noise to a minimum
- Do not write on cave walls

### Safety Rules

1. Never cave alone - use the buddy system
2. Always have three sources of light per person
3. Tell someone your plans and expected return time
4. Stay with your group
5. Move slowly and test each step
6. Watch for loose rocks above
7. Don't jump - climb down carefully
8. Turn back if conditions become dangerous

## Cave Formation

**How caves form:**
Most caves form when slightly acidic water dissolves limestone, dolomite, or other soluble rock over thousands of years. Water follows cracks and weak areas, gradually enlarging them into passages and rooms.

**Types of caves:**
- Solution caves (most common) - formed by chemical weathering
- Lava tubes - formed by flowing lava
- Sea caves - formed by wave action
- Glacier caves - formed in ice

## Cave Life

Caves contain unique ecosystems with specially adapted life forms:
- Bats (use caves for roosting and hibernation)
- Cave fish (often blind and colorless)
- Cave salamanders
- Cave crickets
- Cave spiders
- Microscopic organisms

Many cave animals have adapted to permanent darkness by developing enhanced senses of touch, smell, and hearing while losing pigmentation and sometimes eyesight.

## CAVING GAMES

### Stalactite and Stalagmite Contest
**Equipment:** Tray, two glass jars, saucer, woolen thread, baking soda

**How to play:** Fill jars with water and dissolve as much baking soda as possible. Place jars on tray with saucer between them. Dip twisted thread ends in jars, letting middle hang over saucer. Solution will creep along thread and drip, forming formations.

**Scoring:** Judge after one week on shape, size, and realism.

### Blindfold Maze
**Equipment:** Blindfolds

**How to play:** Groups form circles, each person takes someone else's right hand, then a different person's left hand. While blindfolded, groups try to untangle into straight lines.

**Scoring:** First group to form straight line wins.

### Cave Crawl Obstacle Course
**Equipment:** Tables, chairs, rope, tarps

**How to play:** Create obstacle course simulating cave passages - crawling under tables, through "tight squeezes," around obstacles in darkness.

**Scoring:** Time each participant; award points for completing without touching obstacles.

## MAIN EVENT OPTIONS

### Essential (Tier I): Show Cave Tour
Visit a commercial cave with guided tours, lighting, and developed walkways. Learn about cave formations and geology from experienced guides.

### Challenging (Tier II): Wild Cave Tour
Explore undeveloped sections of caves with proper guide and equipment. Experience crawling through passages and climbing over rocks.

### Advanced (Tier III): Multi-Day Caving Expedition
Spend multiple days exploring different cave systems with overnight camping between locations.

## E.D.G.E. Ideas

### EXPLAIN
- Define different types of caves and how they form
- Explain safety rules and emergency procedures
- Describe proper equipment and its uses
- Discuss cave conservation and ethics

### DEMONSTRATE
- Show proper use of caving equipment
- Demonstrate safe movement techniques
- Show how to change batteries in darkness
- Demonstrate emergency signals and procedures

### GUIDE
- Practice obstacle courses simulating cave passages
- Help Scouts assemble and check equipment
- Guide practice of emergency procedures
- Supervise equipment maintenance activities

### ENABLE
- Have Scouts teach safety rules to others
- Let experienced Scouts assist beginners
- Encourage Scouts to research local caves
- Support Scouts in planning their own cave trips

## Safety Considerations

- All caving activities must be supervised by qualified adult with National Speleological Society credentials
- Weather conditions must be suitable - avoid caves during storm warnings
- Group size should be limited based on cave capacity and safety requirements
- Emergency procedures and communication plans must be established
- First aid supplies and emergency equipment must be available
- Participants must be physically and mentally prepared for underground environment

# COOKING

## Skills for Trail and Home

Good cooking skills make the difference between meals you choke down and meals you can't wait to eat. Whether you're cooking for your family at home, preparing a meal on a camp stove, or creating culinary masterpieces in a Dutch oven over glowing coals, the ability to prepare nutritious, tasty food is one of the most useful skills you can learn.

This month's activities will teach you to be a better cook. You'll learn basic cooking skills and food safety. You'll practice different cooking methods. By the end of the month, your patrol will be ready to prepare a meal that would make a professional chef proud.

### Objectives

This month's activities should:
- Teach Scouts basic cooking skills and food safety
- Help Scouts understand nutritional needs and meal planning
- Give Scouts experience with different cooking methods
- Encourage teamwork in meal preparation
- Help Scouts develop confidence in the kitchen and outdoors

### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

- Scout requirements 2a, 2b, 2c
- Tenderfoot requirements 3a, 3b, 3c, 3d  
- Second Class requirements 2a, 2b, 2c, 2d, 2e, 2f
- First Class requirements 2a, 2b, 2c, 2d
- Cooking merit badge
- Ranger: Outdoor Living core requirement

## Leadership Planning

As a leadership team, discuss these items when choosing cooking as your program feature:

1. What cooking equipment does our unit have available?
2. What is our unit's current cooking skill level?
3. Which cooking methods should we focus on?
4. What food safety training do our leaders have?
5. How can we involve parents with cooking expertise?
6. What advancement opportunities can we incorporate?
7. Where can we practice outdoor cooking safely?
8. How can we accommodate dietary restrictions and food allergies?

## COOKING BASICS

### Food Safety

**Temperature danger zone:** 40°F to 140°F - bacteria multiply rapidly
**Safe minimum internal temperatures:**
- Poultry: 165°F
- Ground meats: 160°F  
- Beef, pork, lamb steaks/chops: 145°F
- Fish: 145°F
- Eggs: 160°F

**Food safety rules:**
- Wash hands before handling food
- Keep raw and cooked foods separate
- Cook to proper temperatures
- Refrigerate perishables within 2 hours (1 hour if over 90°F)
- When in doubt, throw it out

### Basic Cooking Methods

**Boiling:** Cooking in water at 212°F
**Simmering:** Cooking in liquid just below boiling point
**Frying:** Cooking in hot fat or oil
**Baking:** Cooking with dry heat in an oven or Dutch oven
**Grilling:** Cooking over direct heat
**Steaming:** Cooking with moist heat from steam
**Roasting:** Cooking with dry heat, usually for larger items

### Meal Planning

Consider these factors when planning meals:
- Nutritional balance (proteins, carbohydrates, fats, vitamins, minerals)
- Dietary restrictions and allergies
- Available cooking equipment and time
- Storage and refrigeration capabilities
- Weather conditions
- Skill level of cooks
- Cost considerations

## OUTDOOR COOKING METHODS

### Campfire Cooking

**Building a cooking fire:**
- Use hardwoods for long-burning coals
- Let fire burn down to hot coals
- Create different heat zones
- Keep water nearby for safety

**Coal bed cooking:** Push coals to one side for direct heat, use other side for indirect heat

### Dutch Oven Cooking

**Heat distribution:** 
- For baking: 2/3 of coals on lid, 1/3 under oven
- For frying/boiling: Most coals under oven
- For roasting: Coals evenly distributed top and bottom

**Coal count formula:** Diameter of oven + 3 = total briquettes needed

### Camp Stove Cooking

**Advantages:**
- Precise temperature control
- Works in all weather
- Leave No Trace friendly
- Quick and convenient

**Safety rules:**
- Never use stoves inside tents
- Keep fuel away from heat sources
- Check connections before lighting
- Have fire extinguisher available

## COOKING GAMES AND ACTIVITIES

### Iron Chef Competition
**Equipment:** Ingredients, cooking equipment, judges

**How to play:** Teams receive identical ingredients and must create a meal within time limit. Judges rate on taste, presentation, and creativity.

### Blind Taste Test
**Equipment:** Various foods, blindfolds

**How to play:** Blindfolded participants try to identify different foods by taste, smell, and texture.

### Recipe Relay
**Equipment:** Recipe cards, cooking ingredients

**How to play:** Teams race to complete recipes in relay format, with each member completing one step.

### Food Safety Quiz Bowl
**Equipment:** Food safety questions

**How to play:** Teams answer questions about proper food handling, temperatures, and safety procedures.

## E.D.G.E. Ideas

### EXPLAIN
- Demonstrate proper knife skills and safety
- Explain different cooking methods and when to use them
- Discuss food safety principles
- Show how to plan balanced meals

### DEMONSTRATE
- Show proper techniques for building cooking fires
- Demonstrate Dutch oven temperature control
- Show food preparation techniques
- Demonstrate proper food storage methods

### GUIDE
- Help Scouts practice knife skills safely
- Guide meal planning exercises
- Supervise cooking activities
- Help troubleshoot cooking problems

### ENABLE
- Have Scouts teach cooking skills to younger members
- Let Scouts plan and prepare complete meals independently
- Encourage Scouts to try new recipes and techniques
- Support Scouts in earning Cooking merit badge

## MAIN EVENT OPTIONS

### Essential (Tier I): Patrol Cooking Contest
Each patrol prepares a complete meal using camp stoves or Dutch ovens. Focus on basic cooking skills and teamwork.

### Challenging (Tier II): Progressive Dinner
Each patrol prepares one course of a multi-course meal for the entire unit, requiring coordination and timing.

### Advanced (Tier III): Wilderness Cooking Expedition  
Multi-day trip where Scouts must plan, pack, and prepare all meals using lightweight equipment and techniques.

## PARENTS CAN HELP WITH THE COOKING PROGRAM FEATURE BY:

1. Sharing their cooking expertise and favorite recipes
2. Providing ingredients and cooking equipment
3. Supervising food preparation activities
4. Teaching specialized cooking techniques
5. Helping with food shopping and menu planning
6. Maintaining current Youth Protection certification

# COPE

## Challenging Outdoor Personal Experience

COPE is an acronym that stands for Challenging Outdoor Personal Experience. It's an adventure program designed to build confidence, leadership skills, and teamwork through a series of mental and physical challenges. COPE activities include both low elements (ground-based challenges) and high elements (activities conducted on elevated structures).

COPE activities help participants discover their potential by challenging them to attempt what they might have thought impossible. The program emphasizes personal growth, trust building, and overcoming fears in a safe, supportive environment.

### Objectives

This month's activities should:
- Build trust and teamwork among unit members
- Develop problem-solving and leadership skills  
- Challenge Scouts to step outside their comfort zones
- Promote communication and cooperation
- Build self-confidence and self-esteem
- Teach goal setting and achievement

### RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

COPE activities can support advancement in:
- Leadership positions and responsibilities
- Personal fitness requirements
- Teamwork and citizenship development
- Communication skills
- Goal setting and achievement

## Leadership Planning

Consider these items when planning a COPE program:

1. Does our council have a COPE course available?
2. Are there trained COPE facilitators in our area?
3. What is the age and skill level of our participants?
4. How can we incorporate COPE activities into regular programming?
5. What safety equipment and training is required?
6. How can COPE activities support our unit's goals?
7. What follow-up activities will reinforce learning?

## COPE PROGRAM ELEMENTS

### Low COPE Elements

Low elements are ground-based activities that focus on group problem-solving, trust, and communication:

**Trust Fall:** Participant falls backward, trusting team to catch them
**Human Knot:** Group forms tangled "knot" with hands and works to untangle
**Sherpa Walk:** Blindfolded participants guided through obstacle course
**Spider's Web:** Group must pass through web openings without touching
**Nitro Crossing:** Team transports all members across imaginary toxic waste
**Islands:** Group must move between platforms using limited resources
**All Aboard:** Entire group must balance on progressively smaller platforms

### High COPE Elements

High elements are elevated activities that challenge individuals while team provides ground support:

**Pamper Pole:** Climb telephone pole and jump to trapeze
**Giant's Ladder:** Partners climb oversized ladder together  
**Zip Line:** Ride cable from elevated platform to ground
**High Beam:** Walk across elevated beam
**Climbing Wall:** Scale artificial rock wall
**Ropes Course:** Navigate through elevated obstacle course

### Initiative Games

Initiative games are problem-solving activities that require group cooperation:
- Focus on teamwork and communication
- Present challenges with multiple solutions
- Encourage creative thinking
- Build trust and support
- Require planning and coordination

## COPE PRINCIPLES

### Challenge by Choice

Participants choose their level of involvement based on:
- Physical comfort zone
- Emotional readiness  
- Personal goals
- Individual limitations
- Group dynamics

No one is forced to participate in any activity.

### Learning Through Reflection

COPE experiences are processed through guided discussion:
- What happened during the activity?
- What did you learn about yourself/others?
- How does this apply to daily life?
- What will you do differently in the future?

### Safety First

All COPE activities require:
- Trained facilitators
- Proper safety equipment
- Equipment inspection
- Risk assessment
- Emergency procedures
- Participant briefing

## INDOOR INITIATIVE GAMES

### Human Knot
**Equipment:** None needed

**How to play:** Group stands in circle, each person grasps hands of two different people across circle. Group must untangle into circle without releasing hands.

### Trust Circle  
**Equipment:** None needed

**How to play:** One person stands in center of circle with eyes closed, falls in any direction, group gently pushes person back upright.

### Group Juggle
**Equipment:** Various soft objects

**How to play:** Group stands in circle and tosses objects in established pattern, adding more objects while maintaining pattern.

### Blind Polygon
**Equipment:** Rope, blindfolds

**How to play:** Blindfolded group must form perfect square, triangle, or other shape using rope while maintaining contact.

## OUTDOOR INITIATIVE GAMES

### All Aboard
**Equipment:** Platform or tarp

**How to play:** Entire group must balance on platform that gets progressively smaller.

### River Crossing  
**Equipment:** Boards, blocks, or stepping stones

**How to play:** Group must cross imaginary river using limited resources without touching "water."

### Spider Web
**Equipment:** Rope web between trees

**How to play:** Group passes through web openings without touching strands, each opening can only be used once.

### Mine Field
**Equipment:** Blindfolds, various objects as obstacles

**How to play:** Blindfolded partners navigate obstacle course guided only by verbal directions from teammate.

## E.D.G.E. Ideas

### EXPLAIN
- Describe COPE philosophy and goals
- Explain safety procedures and equipment
- Discuss challenge by choice concept
- Outline reflection and processing methods

### DEMONSTRATE  
- Show proper spotting techniques
- Demonstrate equipment use and safety checks
- Model supportive communication
- Show problem-solving strategies

### GUIDE
- Facilitate initiative games and activities
- Help process experiences through reflection
- Support participants through challenges
- Guide group problem-solving efforts

### ENABLE

# SHOTGUN SHOOTING

## 'Pull'

Shotgun shooting is the most basic form of marksmanship. During our nation's early days, many settlers relied on the shotgun to keep meat on the table and provide their home with a measure of security. They had to know how their firearms worked, how to use them skillfully and maintain them in good working order, and how to shoot with utmost respect for the safety of themselves and others.

Shotguns have changed since then. Modern shotguns are reliable and efficient. Some shotgunners shoot at target ranges. Others enjoy the special thrill and challenge of stalking game in the field. Still others aspire to compete on the national or international level.

You won't be ready for the Olympics after this month's meetings and activities. But you will gain a solid understanding of firearm safety and shooting technique while enjoying some exciting target games.

## Objectives

This month's activities should:

- Introduce the rules of safe gun handling.
- Teach Scouts to identify the parts of a shotgun and a shotgun shell.
- Demonstrate the fundamentals of shotgun shooting.
- Let Scouts experience the satisfaction of improving their shooting skills.
- Show Scouts how to react to shotgun shell malfunctions.
- Introduce Scouts to popular games of shotgun shooting.

## RELATED ADVANCEMENT AND AWARDS

- Archery, Rifle Shooting, and Shotgun Shooting merit badges
- Ranger: Shooting Sports elective
- Winchester/NRA Marksmanship Qualification Program awards
- NRA Outstanding Youth Achievement Award
- BSA Shooting Sports Outstanding Achievement Award

---

## Leadership Planning

As a leadership team, you may want to discuss the following items during your planning meetings when choosing shotgun shooting as your program feature.

1. What is our unit's current skill level?
2. Do we have a copy of the current BSA National Shooting Sports Manual for reference?
3. Do we have firearms for demonstrations and what are the BSA rules for handling them?
4. What district or council shooting sports director or NRA shotgun instructor or NRA coach will assist us?
5. Where can we hold meetings and outings?
6. How will we cover the cost of shotgun shells and other expenses?
7. What will we do for our main event?
8. To meet our timeline, what should we change in the sample meeting plans?

## SHOTGUN SHOOTING INFORMATION

### Excerpts From the *Guide to Safe Scouting*

The Boy Scouts of America adheres to its long-standing policy of teaching its youth and adult members the safe, responsible, intelligent handling, care, and use of firearms, air rifles, BB guns, and archery equipment in planned, carefully monitored, age, and supervised programs.

The BSA National Shooting Sports Manual includes all of the information you will need pertaining to permitted goals and the scope of Scouting, the required range supervision, and the training Scouts must have for each activity.

This month's meeting plans assume that you have access to unloaded shotguns and dummy ammunition. If your meeting place does not allow guns, these sessions may need to take place at a different location. Be sure to check the firearms policies of the proposed venue. An instructor must be present.

## PARENTS CAN HELP WITH THE SHOTGUN SHOOTING PROGRAM FEATURE BY:

1. Teaching safe gun handling and firearm skills if they are a currently certified BSA National Camping School shooting sports director OR certified NRA shotgun instructor OR certified NRA coach
2. Pursuing instructor certification if the unit plans to continue doing shotgun activities
3. Arranging for the unit to use the facilities of a Scouting-friendly gun club
4. Providing transportation for the main event
5. Serving as trappers and score-keepers at the main event
6. Maintaining current Youth Protection certification, which is required of all adults providing transportation for or participating in outings

### CONTACT YOUR LOCAL COUNCIL SHOOTING SPORTS COMMITTEE TO:

- Reserve the shooting sports venues at your council camp properties.
- Find a gun club or other facility that will host your unit.
- Seek a certified NRA certification as a shotgun instructor or range safety officer.

## National Rifle Association Rules for Safe Gun Handling

Follow these rules whenever you handle a gun:

**ALWAYS keep the gun pointed in a safe direction.** This is the primary rule of gun safety. A safe direction means that the gun is pointed so that even if it were to go off it would not cause injury or damage. The key to this rule is to control where the muzzle of the gun is pointed at all times. Common sense dictates the safest direction, depending on different circumstances.

**ALWAYS keep your finger off the trigger until ready to fire.** When holding a gun, rest your finger on the trigger guard or along the side of the gun. Until you are actually ready to fire, do not touch the trigger.

**ALWAYS keep the gun unloaded until ready to use.** Whenever you pick up a gun, immediately engage the safety device if possible. If the gun has a magazine, remove it before opening the action and looking into the chamber(s), which should be done only when the gun is pointed in a safe direction. Leave the gun alone and get help from someone who does.

When using or storing a gun, always follow these rules:

**Know your target and what is beyond.** Be absolutely sure you have identified your target beyond any doubt. Equally important, be aware of the area beyond your target. This means observing your surroundings and being aware of whether there are people or any other potential for mishap. Think first. Shoot second.

**Know how to use the gun safely.** Before handling a gun, learn how it operates. Know its basic parts, how to safely open and close the action, and how to remove ammunition such as a clip or a magazine. Remember, a gun's mechanical safety device is not foolproof. Nothing can ever replace safe gun handling.

**Be sure the gun is safe to operate.** Just like other tools, guns need regular maintenance to remain operable. Regular cleaning and proper storage are a part of the gun's general upkeep. If there is any question concerning a gun's ability to function, a knowledgeable gunsmith should look at it.

**Use only the correct ammunition for your gun.** Only BBs, pellets, cartridges, or shells designed for a particular gun can be used safely in that gun. Most guns have the ammunition type stamped on the barrel. Ammunition can be identified by information printed on the box and sometimes stamped on the cartridge. Do not shoot the gun unless you know you have the proper ammunition.

**Wear eye and ear protection at all times.** Guns are loud, and the noise can cause hearing damage. They can also emit debris and hot gas that could cause eye injury. For these reasons, shooting glasses and hearing protectors should be worn by shooters and spectators.

**Never use alcohol or over-the-counter, prescription, or other drugs before or while shooting.** Alcohol, as well as any other substance likely to impair normal mental or physical body functions, must not be used before or while handling shooting guns.

**Store guns so they are not accessible to unauthorized persons.** Dozens of gun storage devices, as well as locking devices that attach directly to the gun, are available. However, mechanical locking devices, like the mechanical safeties built into guns, can all should not be used as a substitute for safe gun handling and the observance of all gun safety rules.

**Be aware that certain types of guns and many shooting activities require additional safety precautions.** For more detailed information, see the *Shotgun Shooting merit badge pamphlet*.

## NOTE TO THE LEADER

Unit leadership must always take responsibility to ensure that all instruction involving any handling of firearms or live ammunition is supervised by the National Camping School shooting sports director or National Rifle Association shotgun instructor or NRA coach.

The shooting standards are established for Scouts and Venturers regarding shotgun shooting. Range operations should, at the same time, both the range safety officer (RSO) and shotgun instructor must be present. The RSO and shotgun instructor may not be the same person. Note that commercial shooting ranges may provide RSOs; see the BSA National Shooting Sports Manual for further details about the shooting sports program. Prerequisite training is available through Boy Scouts of America, at Orientation or NRA Basic Shotgun Course, may need to be conducted in advance depending on the level of the planned activity.

## NOTE TO ADULT LEADERS:

For more information about the BSA's Range and Shooting Sports activities, see:

* National Shooting Sports Manual
* *Fieldbook*
* *Guide to Safe Scouting*

# Shotgun Parts and Ammunition

## Shotgun Parts

**Action.** The mechanism that loads, fires, and ejects shotgun shells. Three common action types are semi-automatic, pump/slide action, and break action.

**Barrel.** The metal tube the shot charge travels through. The bore is the inside diameter of the barrel. Most shotgun barrels are between 18 inches and 30 inches in length.

**Buttstock and forestock.** These parts make up the stock, the wooden or synthetic portions of the gun used to hold and aim the shotgun.

**Choke.** A constriction at the muzzle end of the barrel that controls the spread of shot. Cylinder has no choke. Modified provides moderate choke. Full choke provides maximum choke.

**Trigger and trigger guard.** The trigger, when pulled, releases the firing pin. The trigger guard protects the trigger.

**Safety.** A mechanism that prevents the gun from firing. The safety should be engaged until ready to shoot and immediately reengaged afterward.

## Shotgun Shells

**Hull.** The outer casing, usually made of plastic or paper with a metal base.

**Primer.** A small, sensitive explosive charge that ignites the gunpowder when struck by the firing pin.

**Gunpowder.** The propellant charge that pushes the shot down the barrel.

**Wad.** A plastic or fiber disc that separates the gunpowder from the shot and helps seal the bore.

**Shot.** Small metal pellets. Shot size is indicated by number—the higher the number, the smaller the shot.

---

# Shooting Positions and Techniques

## Four Fundamentals of Shotgun Shooting

1. **Eye dominance.** Determine which eye is dominant and use that eye for aiming.

2. **Shooting position.** Stand with feet shoulder-width apart, body at a slight angle to the target.

3. **Gun mount.** Bring the gun up to your face, not your face down to the gun. The stock should fit snugly against your cheek and shoulder.

4. **Follow-through.** Continue the swing after pulling the trigger. Don't stop the gun when you fire.

## Target Acquisition

**Focus on the target, not the gun.** Keep both eyes open if possible. Point the gun naturally at the target.

**Leading the target.** For moving targets, aim ahead of the target—how far depends on the target's speed and distance.

## Range Commands

All shooters must understand and immediately obey these commands:

**LOAD:** Load shotguns.
**START:** Begin shooting.
**STOP:** Stop shooting immediately.
**UNLOAD:** Unload all shotguns.
**CEASE FIRE:** Stop shooting immediately and make guns safe.

---

# Safety Moments

## MEETING 1: SAFE Assessment

Today we're starting our shotgun shooting program feature. Remember, all firearms activities in Scouting must be supervised by certified instructors. We'll be learning the NRA rules for safe gun handling. The most important rule is to always keep the gun pointed in a safe direction. Always keep your finger off the trigger until ready to shoot. Always keep the gun unloaded until ready to use. These safety rules will become second nature to you.

## MEETING 2: SAFE Equipment

Let's talk about the proper equipment for shotgun shooting. Always wear eye and ear protection when shooting or even when you're near someone else who is shooting. Safety glasses protect your eyes from ejected shells and gun powder residue. Hearing protection prevents hearing damage from the loud noise of gunshots. Never use damaged equipment, and always inspect your gear before use.

## MEETING 3: SAFE Supervision

When we go to the shooting range, qualified adult supervision is required at all times. A certified range safety officer and certified shotgun instructor must both be present. Never handle firearms without proper supervision. The range safety officer's commands must be followed immediately. If you hear "CEASE FIRE," stop shooting immediately and make your gun safe.

## MEETING 4: SAFE Assessment

Before we head to the range for our main event, let's review what makes shooting sports safe. Proper training, certified instruction, appropriate equipment, and following all safety rules and range commands. Never use alcohol or drugs before shooting. Store guns safely when not in use. Know your target and what's beyond it. These principles will keep everyone safe.

# SHOTGUN SHOOTING GAMES

## Shell Identification Relay

**Equipment:** Various dummy shotgun shells, baskets

**How to play:** Teams line up in relay formation. Different types of dummy shells are placed at the opposite end of the room. On command, first team member runs to collect shells and returns. Each shell type scores different points based on difficulty of identification.

**Scoring:** Different shell types score 1-5 points. Team with most points wins.

## Gun Parts Identification

**Equipment:** Disassembled dummy gun parts, identification sheets

**How to play:** Teams rotate through stations identifying different shotgun parts. Each team has 2 minutes per station to identify as many parts as possible.

**Scoring:** 1 point per correctly identified part. Highest total wins.

## Target Call

**Equipment:** Pictures of different clay target presentations

**How to play:** Show pictures of various clay target flight patterns. Teams must identify the type of shot and proper lead required.

**Scoring:** Correct identification and technique scores points.

## Safety Rules Review

**Equipment:** Scenario cards

**How to play:** Teams draw scenario cards describing potentially unsafe situations. They must identify safety violations and proper procedures.

**Scoring:** Correct identification of all safety issues scores points.

- Allow experienced Scouts to help teach newcomers.
- Encourage Scouts to pursue further shooting sports training.
- Enable Scouts to identify different types of shotguns and shells.
- Allow Scouts to explain safety procedures to others.

# FUNDAMENTALS OF SHOTGUN SHOOTING

## Shooting Position (Stance)
Your shooting position must be relaxed and comfortable. Do not rest your muscles. Place your feet firmly on the ground about shoulder-width apart. Your front knee should be bent slightly while your back leg stays relatively straight. This position provides good balance and the ability to move.

## Sight Alignment (Gun-Ready Position)
This is the position you hold while waiting for the target to appear. Maintain your basic shooting stance, with your trigger hand placed on the grip of the stock and your nontrigger hand at about the middle of the shotgun forearm. The grip of both hands should be snug but not tight as to create strain. Your cheek should rest lightly on the stock, placing your face on the front side of your ribs. The muzzle is placed lightly, below the target.

Both eyes should be open and focused in the area where you expect the target to appear.

## Swing to Target
On first seeing the target, quickly swing your gun into position for a smooth swing and hit or snap shot on the gun and the target. To achieve this position:

* Keep your eyes focused on the target at all times.
* Position your face firmly against the stock.
* Bring the trigger hand elbow into position about level with the shoulder.
* Place the butt of the stock against your shoulder.

With correct gun fit, the barrel will be aligned in front of your dominant eye and with the target.

*Note: Unlike lining up the sights of a rifle, sighting is more of a pointing motion with your shotgun.*

## Trigger Pull
This should take place at the instant when, looking at the moving target, you see your gun's muzzle touch it. Timing and reflex are essential; your pull must be quick and crisp, but be careful not to jerk the gun.

## Follow-Through
Except for targets flying absolutely straightaway from the shooter, the shotgun muzzle must move through the target. The trigger is pulled while the shotgun is moving, and the gun must continue to move after the shot is fired. (Stopping the motion of the gun after touching the target is the most common cause of misses by beginning shotgunners.) The shotgun must remain welded to your body, especially the cheek.

## Skeet
In skeet shooting, targets are cast away from the shooters at various angles. Targets can cross, come straight toward you, or move away from you. Shooters stand in a semicircular field with multiple stations, and they move from one station to the next. Targets are thrown by two machines. The "high house" launches targets from a point high off the ground. The "low house" launches targets from somewhere around waist level. From each station, the shooter shoots one target from each house. Positions 1, 2, 6, and 7 include "doubles" where targets are thrown from the high and low houses simultaneously, and the shooter fires two shots. The 25th shot is fired when a shooter scores the first miss.

## Trap
In trap shooting with multiple shooters, they stand in a line at about semicircle, side by side, as moving targets are launched from a partially underground bunker. Targets are thrown 16 yards in front of the firing line at about 42 mph and in a consistent vertical angle to simulate wild birds flying away from a hunter. The targets are cast in random directions within a 45-degree angle on either side relative to the line.

A squad of five shooters competes in a round with each shooter starting at one of five stations, or posts. Shooters alternate positions after each shot, rotating from their starting position. Shooters shift one position to the right until they have shot at each station.

## Sporting Clays
In sporting clays, each shot is different. A typical course includes 10 to 15 stations winding through woods and fields. Machines launch clays from each station at unpredictable angles, sometimes sending two targets at once to simulate a "true pair" of birds—or a "report pair," which would launch the second bird after the first shot. Other clays may fly straight toward you from a clump of bushes on the ground to simulate a rabbit on the run.

## Five Stand
In five stand shooting, there are more crossing and flying targets than in trap, but less than in sporting clays. The clays are thrown to each participant's position on the left, right, or straight ahead of the five shooting stations, or even from behind the shooter. Each shooter gets five target "presentations" at each station for a round of 25 shots. As an alternative, try playing Scouting clays, a modified five stand game where the focus is on going-away targets at less than 20 degrees; a wobble trap might be used in place of the machine.

## Safety Moments

### MEETING 1: SAFE Equipment
When handling firearms, always remember the three fundamental rules:
- ALWAYS keep the gun pointed in a safe direction
- ALWAYS keep finger off trigger until ready to shoot
- ALWAYS keep gun unloaded until ready to use
These rules are the cornerstone of firearm safety, and they're crucial to follow every time you're on the range. Listen carefully to instructors and follow their directions. Safety is our top priority.

### MEETING 2: SAFE Equipment
It's essential to wear appropriate safety gear when shooting. Make sure that you have proper eye and ear protection to shield yourself from potential hazards. Wear clothing that fits well and won't get caught on anything. Check your equipment before you begin. Inspect your equipment and ammunition to ensure everything is in good working order. Remember, safety starts with you! If you see someone acting unsafely or if you're unsure about something, speak up. A culture of safety is everyone's responsibility, and together we can create a safe and enjoyable environment for everyone participating in shooting sports.

### MEETING 3: SAFE Supervision
Competent adult leadership is an essential part of shooting sports safety. If you see someone acting unsafely or you're unsure about something, don't hesitate to pause and talk carefully to instructions from your range master or instructor and follow them without hesitation. Our range masters and instructors are trained to ensure a successful and safe experience. Remember, safety is a shared responsibility, and we all play a role in ensuring that every Scout has both a safe and respectful atmosphere. Let's work together to uphold the Boy Scouts of America's shooting sports guidelines and make every shooting activity a success.

### MEETING 4: SAFE Fitness and Skills
Before you start shooting, familiarize yourself with the range rules and procedures. Pay attention to any safety briefing provided by the range master. Follow all range commands promptly and without hesitation. If you have any questions or concerns, don't hesitate to ask for clarification. Listening to the range officers is an important skill each of us must use during shooting activities. Remember, your attentiveness and respect play a role in ensuring a safe and enjoyable experience on the shooting range.

## SAFE SWIM DEFENSE REQUIREMENTS

### 1. Qualified Supervision
Focuses on responsible and qualified adult supervision (age 21 or over) trained in BSA Safe Swim Defense.

### 2. Personal Health Review
Addresses medical complications.

### 3. Safe Area
Concerns controlled access and conditions of the venue, and having the proper equipment on hand.

### 4. Response Personnel (Lifeguards)
Ensures someone is available to provide safe and effective assistance.

### 5. Lookout
Ensures the eyes and ears are on alert for trouble.

### 6. Ability Groups
Matches activities, areas, and equipment to the swimmer's ability.

### 7. Buddy System
Provides an extra layer of protection for each participant.

### 8. Discipline
Reinforces each participant's knowledge, understanding, and respect regarding safe swimming as outlined in the Safe Swim Defense guidelines.

## SWIMMING BUDDY SYSTEM

Every participant is paired with another. Buddies check into and out of the area together. They stay together, monitor each other, and alert the safety team if either needs assistance or is missing. Buddies are normally in the same ability group and remain in their assigned area. If they are not in the same ability group, then they swim in the area assigned to the buddy with the lesser ability.

A buddy check reminds participants of their obligation to monitor their buddies and indicates how closely the buddies are keeping track of each other. Roughly every 10 minutes, or as needed to keep the buddies together, the look-out, or another person designated by the supervisor, gives an audible signal, such as a single whistle blast, and a call to "Buddies!" All swimmers are asked to raise each other's hand before completion of a slow, audible count to 10. Buddies who take longer to find each other should be reminded of their responsibility for the other's safety.

## SWIMMING SAFETY REQUIREMENTS

All participants of BSA swimming activities must follow Safe Swim Defense. Adult leaders supervising a swimming activity must have completed Safe Swim Defense training within the previous two years. Safe Swim Defense standards apply at all aquatic areas including BSA camps and council-established waterfront areas such as beaches at state parks and U.S. Army Corps of Engineers lakes; and at all temporary swimming areas such as a lake, river, or ocean. Safe Swim Defense does not apply to boating or water activities such as waterskiing or swamped boat drills that are covered by Safety Afloat guidelines.

Safe Swim Defense applies to other nonswimming activities whenever participants enter water over knee deep or when submersion is likely, for example, when fording a stream, seining for bait, or constructing a bridge as a pioneering project.

Swim tests are required for participants who wish to have demonstrated knowledge and skills equivalent to those for Scouting BSA in addition to following Safe Swim Defense. Because of concerns with hyperventilation, competitive underwater swimming events are not permitted in Scouting.

## WINTER CAMPING CLOTHING PRINCIPLES

### Wick
Your base layer should wick moisture away from your skin. Cotton can actually pull moisture toward your base layer, because it will trap moisture and make you colder. Also avoid cotton socks.

### Wind
Your outermost layer should block the wind.

### Warm
Your middle layer or layers should trap the heat that your body generates.

## SNOW SHELTER CONSTRUCTION

### Choosing the Site
Choose a spot that is free of any avalanche danger and that provides a flat and stable base. Look for a drift or swale near ridges or trees. A shelter with its entrance on the leeward side of a slope will offer additional protection from the wind. Form snow on a pile at least 5 feet deep. If snow is too shallow, you may need to dig large enough to accommodate the number of people to be sheltered.

### Tunneling In
Before tunneling, it is important that you are not working alone. Use the buddy system and keep a watcher outside to help in case of emergencies. Have an adult present. In deep snow, start by digging a trench downward. As you dig, snow that is being removed to form the trench should be placed on the roof area of the shelter. Stomp on the snow to make it hard and compact.

### Shaping the Shelter
The shelter walls should be at least 12 inches thick to create a stable and well-insulated structure. Provide a dome-shaped space at the top of the tunnel. An elevated sleeping platform is important because it puts you closer to the warmer air in the upper part of the shelter.

### Adding Finishing Touches
Smooth the curved roof to remove any sharp edges that might cause moisture to drip onto your gear as the temperature in the shelter rises. Use a ski pole, shovel handle, or stick to punch several ventilation holes in the ceiling at a 45-degree angle to the floor. Leave about 6 inches between the holes and check them at regular intervals if they become clogged.

## BSA SHOTGUN SHOOTING TRAINING REQUIREMENTS

### Level 2 Ongoing Shooting Sports
Refer to the Level 2 Ongoing Shooting Sports requirements outlined in chapter 1 of the BSA National Shooting Sports Manual. The 30-minute safety briefing must be presented by an NRA shotgun instructor prior to the shooting activity.

### Level 3 Ongoing Shooting Sports Program
Training: NRA Shotgun FIRST Steps (three hours) or NRA Basics of Shotgun Shooting (course taught in four hours) taught by a National Camping School shooting instructor or a certified NRA instructor.

### Supervision Requirements
One certified NRA range safety officer must directly supervise all live fire on the shotgun field. Additionally, one NRA shotgun instructor for each active shooter is required when loading and firing a shotgun.

## BASIC SWIMMING STROKES

### Front Crawl
The front crawl has three parts: the flutter kick, the trudgen arm stroke, and rhythmic breathing. It is the fastest swimming stroke. The flutter kick begins at the hips and flows to the feet. Most of the forward motion comes from the arm stroke, which has three phases: catch, power, and recovery. Swimmers use a breathing rhythm of one breath for every two, three, or more arm cycles.

### Breaststroke
The breaststroke conserves energy and can keep their head above water for longer distances. It uses a whip kick and a shallow arm pull. The breaststroke begins in the prone glide position with your arms and legs straight. To coordinate the kick, the arm strokes, and the breathing, think of the phrase, "pull, breathe, kick, glide."

### Sidestroke
The sidestroke is a good long-distance stroke with a long, restful glide. It uses the scissors kick and has a long resting period between arm strokes. The arm stroke has catch, power, and recovery phases. To coordinate arms and legs, remember the phrase, "pull, kick, glide."

### Elementary Backstroke
The elementary backstroke is a restful stroke good for swimming longer periods. It uses the whip kick while floating on your back. The arms and legs don't provide power at the same time. Begin the recovery of the arms before the legs.

## SUSTAINABILITY PRINCIPLES

### Definition of Sustainability
"Development that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs." People concerned about sustainable development focus on the triple bottom line of people, planet, and profit.

### The Seven R's

**Reduce:** Choose organic foods, sustainable products, and products that come with minimal packaging or packaging that can be recycled.

**Reuse:** When buying everything from furniture to clothing to electronics, consider buying used instead of new.

**Recycle:** Recycle everything you can, including paper, plastic, glass, metal, and batteries. Find places that will accept exotic plastics and other hard-to-recycle items.

**Repurpose:** Get creative. Turn a shipping pallet into a bookcase, turn an old bicycle into a garden planter.

**Refuse:** Vote with your dollars. Don't buy products that have a negative impact on natural resources.

**Rethink:** Ask yourself whether you really need the latest tablet or smartphone.

**Repair:** Choose products that can be repaired, and learn how to repair them.

### Renewable vs. Nonrenewable Resources

**Renewable resources** grow or replace themselves over some period of time. They include wind, solar, agricultural harvests, trees, water, and air.

**Nonrenewable resources** will not replace themselves when depleted. They include coal, petroleum products, minerals, and land.

## SKATEBOARDING SAFETY RULES

### Unwritten Rules of Skateboarding
1. Never skate alone, and always have an emergency plan.
2. Always check the area for possible dangers before skating.
3. Skate within your limits.
4. Wear all required protective equipment.
5. Learn how to fall safely.
6. Learn basic skateboard techniques before going to a skate park.
7. Do not stop, stand, or sit on skate features.
8. Communicate with other users.

### Safety Equipment
Always wear a dual-certified (ASTM/CPSC) helmet when skateboarding. Knee pads and elbow pads are suggested for all skill levels. Wrist guards are typically recommended for beginner- and intermediate-level skateboarders.

### How to Fall
When learning how to fall, it is almost always better to fall forward as opposed to falling backward. When executing a "bail" or a controlled fall, it is typically better to fall and go with the motion than it is to try to prevent yourself from falling by trying to stick out an arm or leg to brace yourself.

## TECHNOLOGY AND CYBER SAFETY

### The Scout Law and Cybersafety

**Trustworthy:** Be truthful with others online, and be very careful of the information you share.

**Loyal:** Share information about others only if you have their permission to share it.

**Helpful:** Alert others to scams, cheats, and suspicious sites. Point them to reliable and accurate sources of information.

**Friendly:** Reach out to support others who are doing good things. Support those who are bullied.

**Courteous:** Be polite and respectful. When you use other people's work, be sure to ask permission when needed.

**Kind:** Treat people with respect when you are on social networks, playing games, or in other digital activities.

**Obedient:** Follow the rules set by your parents/guardians, teachers, and Scout leaders. Abide by the rules established by sites, services, devices, and games.

**Cheerful:** Use games, messaging tools, and social forums to build your relationships with others while having fun.
